Preface 
This Master Thesis was written by Ann Sofie Grube Kjeldsen, s184535, at the Technical University 
of Denmark, DTU. This project was the final project of the M.Sc. in Architectural Engineering. The 
thesis was carried out in the period of 28/08/2023 – 16/02/2024. Throughout this period, I have gained 
valuable insights and practical experiences related to industry practices. The exploration of window 
reuse proved to be a complex and intriguing subject, and I extend my gratitude to VELUX for provid-
ing me with the opportunity to delve deeper into this complex topic. 
This report has been written under the supervision of Carsten Rode, Professor in the Section for Ma-
terials and Durability. Special thanks to Carsten Rode for his guidance, mentorship, and support 
throughout this research. Also, a special thanks to, Lucile Sarran, my co-supervisor from VELUX for 
her insights, perspectives, and dedicated involvement, which has played a role in enhancing the com-
prehensiveness of this research. I am equally grateful to Anja Gylling, my second co-supervisor, for 
adding her perspective and expertise to the thesis, which has been valuable for the content of my 
project. Furthermore, I would like to thank Jan Fransson for his assistance and for generously allow-
ing me to utilize VELUX’s test facilities to conduct performance tests on used windows.  
Finally, I extend my gratitude to the numerous companies and participants for their substantial input 
to my research. I sincerely appreciate the insights shared. Their generosity in sharing knowledge and 
experiences has played a crucial role in shaping this study.

==PAGE:5==
v 
 
Abstract 
This thesis investigates the sustainability implications of utilizing reclaimed windows in construction 
practices. With a growing emphasis on sustainable building materials and practices, the reuse of ex-
isting building components, such as windows, presents a promising opportunity to reduce environ-
mental impact and promote principles of the circular economy. The environmental, economic, and 
performance aspects of reclaimed roof windows are examined through a comprehensive analysis, 
including testing of the thermal performance of recycled windows and a case study on life cycle 
assessment and life cycle costs. Additionally, the current implications of reuse and recycling in the 
construction industry are investigated to provide a broader perspective on barriers and opportunities. 
The study investigates the environmental impact of maintenance and recycling content in windows 
compared to entirely new window construction materials using life cycle assessment. The results 
indicate significant reductions in the environmental footprint associated with recycled window com-
ponents, highlighting their potential as sustainable alternatives. A life cycle cost analysis provides 
insights into the economic sustainability of reusing and refurbishing windows compared to purchas-
ing new ones. Additionally, tests for thermal performance were conducted to evaluate the thermal 
efficiency, air tightness, and structural integrity of reclaimed roof windows. The results demonstrate 
the practical importance of maintenance measures to preserve the effectiveness of windows over time 
and identify challenges and opportunities associated with the reuse of roof windows. The analysis 
also reveals the role of stakeholders in the construction industry and their approach to the reuse and 
recycling of building materials, emphasizing the need for streamlined testing methods and quality 
standards to promote reuse. Along with a cultural shift towards sustainability, this can contribute to a 
greener future in construction practices. 
The thesis concludes with recommendations for further studies and practical implications for stake-
holders in the construction industry, including the development of a tool and guide based on the re-
sults. These resources can be valuable for those wishing to maintain the thermal performance of win-
dows or reuse and recycle them while addressing key challenges and promoting sustainable practices. 
The adoption of reclaimed windows can thus contribute to a greener future in construction practices 
and be in line with global sustainability goals. 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:6==
vi 
 
Resumé 
Denne afhandling undersøger bæredygtighedsimplikationerne ved at anvende genanvendte vinduer i 
byggepraksis. Med en voksende vægt på bæredygtige byggematerialer og praksisser præsenterer gen-
anvendelsen af eksisterende bygningskomponenter, såsom vinduer, en lovende mulighed for at redu-
cere miljøpåvirkningen og fremme principperne om cirkulær økonomi. De miljømæssige, økonomi-
ske og præstationsmæssige aspekter af genanvendte vinduer udforskes gennem en omfattende ana-
lyse, der omfatter test af genbrugte vinduers termiske ydeevne, samt et casestudie om livscyklusvur-
dering, livscyklusomkostninger. Derudover undersøges de aktuelle implikationer forbundet med gen-
brug og genanvendelse i byggebranchen for at give et bredere perspektiv på barrierer og muligheder. 
Studiet undersøger miljøpåvirkningen af vedligeholdelse og genanvendelsesindhold i vinduer sam-
menlignet med helt nye vinduesbyggematerialer ved hjælp af livscyklusvurdering. Resultaterne viser 
store miljømæssige reduktioner ved genanvendte vindueselementer, hvilket understreger deres poten-
tiale som bæredygtige alternativer. Samtidig viser en livscyklusomkostningsanalyse de økonomiske 
fordele og ulemper ved at genbruge og renovere vinduer i forhold til at købe nye. Yderligere blev der 
udført test for termisk ydeevne for at evaluere den termiske effektivitet, lufttæthed og integritet af 
genbrugte tagvinduer. Resultaterne demonstrerer den praktiske betydning af vedligeholdelsesforan-
staltninger for at bevare vinduernes effektivitet over tid og identificere udfordringer og muligheder 
forbundet med genbrug af tagvinduer. Analysen afslører desuden interessenternes rolle i bygningsin-
dustrien og deres tilgang til genbrug og genanvendelse af byggematerialer, hvilket understreger be-
hovet for strømlinede testmetoder og kvalitetsstandarder for at fremme genanvendelse. Sammen med 
en kulturel forandring mod bæredygtighed kan dette bidrage til en grønnere fremtid inden for bygge-
praksis. 
Afhandlingen afsluttes med anbefalinger til yderligere studier og praktiske implikationer for interes-
senter i byggeindustrien, herunder udviklingen af et værktøj og en guide baseret på resultaterne. Disse 
ressourcer kan være værdifulde for dem, der ønsker at vedligeholde vinduernes termiske ydeevne 
eller genbruge og genanvende dem, samtidig med at de adresserer centrale udfordringer og fremmer 
bæredygtige praksisser. Vedtagelsen af genanvendte vinduer kan derfor bidrage til en grønnere frem-
tid inden for byggepraksis og være i overensstemmelse med globale bæredygtighedsmål.

==PAGE:15==
Chapter 1.  Introduction 
 
1 
 
Chapter 1. Introduction 
The European construction industry significantly impacts energy consumption and emissions, con-
tributing to 42% of final energy consumption, 35% of greenhouse gas emissions, and utilizing over 
50% of all extracted materials [1], [2]. Legislative tools have primarily addressed building energy 
demands, but material intensity has increased with population growth, leading to nearly a third of all 
EU waste volume from construction and demolition waste [1]. Managing this waste is crucial but 
challenging, and a shift toward sustainable renovation and retrofit strategies is necessary [2]. How-
ever, the construction industry's focus on new buildings and traditional practices poses a hurdle to 
this shift [2].  
In recent years, despite efforts to control global warming, temperatures have increased, reaching 1.15 
˚C in 2022, exceeding the estimated values [3]. Two-thirds of this increase has occurred at a rate of 
0.15˚C – 0.20˚C per decade since 1975 [3], [4]. The root of these global changes lies in resource 
extraction and waste disposal driven by high material consumption, due to population growth, eco-
nomic growth, and changing consumption habits [5]. The future construction landscape demands ma-
terials and resources beyond Earth's resource capacity, with an anticipated need to construct 230 bil-
lion square meters of new buildings over the next 40 years [5]. This path suggests an ongoing rise in 
material consumption, impacting climate change, biodiversity, and ecosystem degradation, necessi-
tating immediate measures [6]. 
Despite this inspected increase in material consumption, the sector continues to produce a substantial 
amount of waste, highlighting the ongoing development of strategies for reuse and recycling. Cur-
rently, the sector generates five million tons of waste annually, constituting 35% of Denmark's overall 
waste volume [7], [8]. Resource waste during execution amounts to approx. 10-15% of total material 
consumption [7], [9], and 54% of demolition waste is landfilled due to destructive practices or tox-
icity. The current exploration of resources, waste patterns, waste disposal, and related emissions leads 
to critical levels of climate change and degradation [5]. If the material consumption rate continues, it 
will be impossible to keep global warming below 2˚C [3]. 
Addressing this challenge involves a fundamental shift towards the reuse and recycling of materials, 
diverting from reliance on new ones [10]. A substantial portion of what is currently designated as 
waste in Denmark holds the potential for repurposing in construction [5], [7]. A holistic strategy to 
mitigate environmental issues entails a global reduction in material consumption and an enhancement 
of resource efficiency [4], [11]. To navigate this, it is vital to engage with current market dynamics 
surrounding reclaimed building materials.  
Presently, the market for reclaimed materials is primarily influenced by private consumers [8]. Scal-
ing the use of reclaimed materials on a larger scale, involving both professionals and public entities 
in the construction sector is imperative to reduce reliance on new building materials. Another crucial 
approach is to redefine existing buildings as material banks, rather than viewing them as waste [12]. 
However, the adoption of reclaimed building materials is only a potential alternative in the construc-
tion industry if the accessibility, visibility, and supply are increased [8]. Only a few high-value 
==PAGE:16==
Chapter 1.  Introduction 
 
2 
 
components are reclaimed and traded by the salvage industry [12], and the shift towards reuse de-
mands a completely new mindset and innovative approaches throughout the construction value chain 
[8]. Although the construction industry acknowledges the benefits of reclaimed building materials, 
challenges arise due to the affordability and efficiency of new materials, affecting their competitive-
ness in terms of pricing, ordering, and delivery [5], [7].  
A high-value product, such as windows is a frequently replaced building component, often due to 
significant heat loss over time. The window is one of the primary components of a building that has 
a great impact on energy consumption since the heat loss can range from 20% to 30% [13], [14]. 
Additionally, restoring the energy efficiency of windows after they have experienced a decline in 
thermal performance is a more intricate task compared to insulating exterior walls or roofs. Sustaining 
the thermal efficiency of windows can pose challenges, often requiring replacement instead. There-
fore, these products have an urgent need for improved competitiveness and efficiency in refurbish-
ment, reuse, and recycling initiatives, compared to the use of new windows. This highlights the im-
portance of developing strategies and guidance to promote sustainable and responsible construction 
practices. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:17==
Chapter 1.  Introduction 
 
3 
 
1.1 Problem statement and objectives  
The thesis aims to identify sustainable practices related to window reuse in building construction. A 
deeper understanding of the technical, environmental, and economic challenges associated with win-
dow reuse will be investigated, through testing of window performance, interviews with different 
stakeholders, and case studies. The focus of the report is to guide on assessing the feasibility of win-
dow reuse and recycling, offering practical instructions for maintenance, improving thermal perfor-
mance, and considerations for larger projects. 
Two thesis objectives were created: 
• gain insight into the sustainable practices and implications associated with the integration of 
reclaimed windows in the construction industry,  
• provide practical guidance for assessing the feasibility of window reuse and recycling. 
The first objective aims to delve into sustainable practices and their implications concerning the in-
corporation of reclaimed windows within the construction industry. This involves comprehensively 
understanding the processes, challenges, and benefits related to integrating reclaimed windows into 
construction projects from an environmental, economic, and technical standpoint. The objective seeks 
to analyse the sustainability implications of utilizing reclaimed windows and their potential impact 
on construction practices, thereby providing valuable insights for promoting sustainable construction 
methodologies. 
The second objective aims to develop practical methods for evaluating the viability of reusing and 
recycling windows in construction. It involves identifying weak components in recycled windows 
through comprehensive testing, facilitating informed decision-making in construction projects. Addi-
tionally, the objective seeks to enhance understanding of recycled window performance to promote 
their effective reuse, thus advancing sustainable construction practices. 
 
1.2 Research questions and hypotheses 
This section introduces the hypotheses and associated research questions exploring various aspects 
of the research. They aim to uncover valuable insights and contribute to a deeper understanding of 
the context of the study.  
Hypothesis A: maintenance and repurposing have a direct positive impact on a window’s life cycle 
costs and environmental impacts. 
A.1 What is the environmental impact of using recycled or reused materials compared to vir-
gin materials? 
A.2 In what ways does maintenance contribute to the environmental footprint of windows, 
and how does this influence their lifespan? 
Hypothesis B: by testing the thermal performances of existing roof windows it is possible to identify 
the weakest components. 
==PAGE:18==
Chapter 1.  Introduction 
 
4 
 
B.1 How do different components of existing windows contribute to their thermal perfor-
mance, and can testing methods effectively identify the weakest components in terms of 
thermal efficiency? 
B.2 How can the identification of these weakest components contribute to targeted improve-
ments in windows efficiency? 
Hypothesis C: the background and practices linked to different stakeholders in the building process 
will influence the nature of challenges and opinions related to the reuse of windows. 
C.1 How do the roles and positions of different stakeholders involved influence their perspec-
tives and challenges regarding the reuse of building materials and windows? 
C.2 What are the specific challenges and opinions among stakeholders in the building process 
regarding the reutilization of windows, and how do these factors affect decision-making 
processes and strategies? 
 
1.3 Thesis structure  
Chapter 1. Introduction: This chapter provides an overview of the topic and the need for sustainable 
solutions to slow the rate of emissions. The hypothesis and research questing, problem statement, and 
objectives.  
Chapter 2. Literature review: This is a comprehensive review of existing literature related to win-
dow reuse. It investigates regulatory aspects, challenges, and methods, and explores factors influenc-
ing the life span and quality of used windows.  
Chapter 3. Methodology of the field studies: The research approach includes a case study, interview 
study, and window performance testing to gather insights into reuse, performance, cost, and environ-
mental impact. It examines existing solutions for window reuse, recycling, and maintenance, evalu-
ating economic and environmental aspects for a comprehensive understanding of sustainability im-
plications. Additionally, a qualitative study explores industry practices related to recycling, window 
reuse, and broader aspects of the circular economy, revealing motivations, challenges, and perspec-
tives. Finally, thermal performance testing is conducted on reclaimed VELUX windows aged 15 
years, involving visual inspection, thermal transmittance, and air permeability tests to assess their 
potential for effective reuse. 
Chapter 5. Discussion: The discussion encompasses insights gathered from a multifaceted research 
approach, including a case study, interview study, and window performance testing. Economic and 
environmental evaluations contribute to understanding sustainability implications. Additionally, qual-
itative investigation delves into industry practices related to recycling, window reuse, and the broader 
circular economy, uncovering motivations, challenges, and perspectives. Thermal performance test-
ing of reclaimed VELUX windows investigates potential for reuse, including air permeability with 
SD analysis to develop reliable testing methods. Additionally, U-value assessment ensures compli-
ance with energy balance regulations. 
==PAGE:19==
Chapter 1.  Introduction 
 
5 
 
Chapter 6. How to reuse and recycle windows: Provides a preliminary map for reusing and recy-
cling windows, designed to guide both consultants and individuals seeking to assess the feasibility of 
window reuse. It includes a guide on the effects of recycling and reuse of windows, focusing on the 
potential for window maintenance and reuse. This guide offers instructions on maintaining windows 
to improve air permeability and outlines considerations for reusing windows in larger projects, mak-
ing it applicable to the industry as well as individuals. 
Chapter 7. Conclusion: The conclusion summarizes key findings on sustainable window reuse, 
maintenance impact, and thermal performance. It also discusses the influence of stakeholders in con-
struction practices and emphasizes the need for a balanced approach. Overall, it underscores the im-
portance of regular assessment and maintenance for ensuring sustainability in building practices. 
Chapter 8. Further research: The section proposes expanding stakeholder engagement and collab-
orating with industry experts to explore window reuse further. It suggests investigating thermal per-
formance, solar energy dynamics, and optimal storage conditions for reclaimed windows. Addition-
ally, conducting lifecycle assessments is recommended to evaluate the environmental and financial 
benefits of window reuse.
==PAGE:20==
6 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Chapter 2.  Background 
This section will investigate existing industry practices and technologies, assessing the viability of 
reusing components and windows along with the associated implications. The first section will intro-
duce the primary principles within the concept of circular economy and the waste hierarchy (2.1). 
The second section will uncover primary technologies and concepts within sustainable practices, such 
as urban mining, design for disassembly, demolition, and deconstruction processes (2.2). The next 
section delves into the potential of reusing windows, including an investigation of durability, its life 
cycle, and reuse strategies developed by companies in the construction industry (2.3). Finally, the last 
section aims to uncover the persistent obstacles and challenges that currently block effective reuse in 
the building sector (2.4). The aim is to identify relevant theories and practices in the industry that 
could potentially contribute to the development of new guidelines towards window reuse.

2.1 Circular economy and the construction and demolition waste 
With mounting concerns about the environment and limited resources, the building industry has the 
potential to incorporate Circular Economy (CE) principles [1], [15], [16]. While there is no univer-
sally accepted definition of a CE, strategies such as reduction, reuse, repair, refurbishment, and recy-
cling are widely advocated [9]. However, implementing these strategies presents challenges due to 
the complexity of processes, stakeholders, and the extended lifespan of building components, neces-
sitating new market mechanisms and collaborations across the value chain [9]. Central to the transi-
tion to a CE is the concept of adaptive reuse, with the rise of closed-loop (products are continuously 
recycled or reused) and cradle-to-cradle (creation of products that can be recycled or biodegraded) 
approaches [15]. Enhancing material reuse and addressing concerns such as quality loss and meeting 
energy requirements requires the development of user-friendly and scientifically sound methods [5], 
[15]. By incorporating secondary materials through material reuse in construction projects, the em-
bedded impacts of primary production can be decreased [5]. However, developing effective solutions 
at a reasonable cost poses a challenge, and requires innovation and investments [16]. 
 
Figure 1 Waste hierarchy and the order of waste strategies in waste management [17] 
The hierarchy of waste management, seen in Figure 1, prioritizes reusing materials before disposing 
of them [1]. To reach its potential, obstacles such as the uncertainty of secondary material prices and 
high costs have to be overcome [16]. According to the waste hierarchy, the lowest level involves 
disposal, while the top strategies include recovery, recycling, preparation for reuse, and waste pre-
vention [1]. Waste prevention focuses on minimizing waste generation, and actively reducing waste 
during project design [1]. Preparation for reuse involves cleaning or repairing materials while recy-
cling requires sorting and melting for secondary use.  
Given that the buildings account for 35% of waste in DK, structured waste management is imperative 
[18], [19]. Challenges such as limited opportunities, selective demolition, and purity requirements 
hinder the ease of reusing building components [20]. Embracing the principles of the waste hierarchy, 
reduce, reuse, and recycle, can provide a framework for optimizing resource utilization and minimiz-
ing waste generation in the construction industry. 
 
==PAGE:22==
Chapter 1. Background of the study  
 
8 
 
2.2 Integration of sustainable practices and technologies 
2.2.1   Deconstruction and demolition technologies 
The current market preference leans toward demolition over deconstruction, primarily due to obsta-
cles like safety concerns and time constraints associated with the deconstruction process. Effectively 
managing Construction and Demolition Waste (CDW) is crucial in the establishment of a framework 
for deconstruction. In demolition, building materials are recklessly destroyed, making components 
unusable [21]. In contrast, deconstruction (selective demolition) involves the selective disassembly 
of components, ensuring recyclability and reuse.  
As indicated by [21], deconstruction offers a cost-efficient means to recover components and materi-
als for reuse or recycling. Despite its advantages, deconstruction is still undergoing scrutiny in terms 
of political and design considerations. In addition, construction materials' current unstructured design 
makes it more time-consuming when compared to demolition. [21] delves into the various benefits, 
shortcomings, and challenges associated with the technologies of building demolition and decon-
struction, as described in Table 1. 
Table 1 Comparison between selective demolition and conventional demolition [21] 
Methods 
Benefits 
Shortcomings and challenges 
Conventional demolition 
Timesaving 
More waste 
 
Simple when using heavy machinery 
Makes more noise 
Selective demolition 
Extension of lifetime of raw materials 
Time-consuming 
 
Reduction of potential pollution from 
new materials and structures 
Lack of reasonable design methods if the 
materials are designed without DfD 
 
Reduce disposal cost for old structures 
Not cost-effectiveness in terms of labour 
 
2.2.2    Design for disassembly and associated technologies 
The deconstruction of buildings is vital for supporting Design for Disassembly (DfD), a key element 
in a circular economy. DfD is an approach in product design where products are created to make them 
easy to disassemble for repair, recycling, or reuse. The strategy aims to minimize waste and promote 
sustainability throughout a product's lifecycle. Despite DfD being well-established in consumer prod-
ucts, its integration in the construction industry lags [21]. It highlights the importance of choosing 
toxic-free, high-quality materials, and prioritizing those with high recycling potential. Nevertheless, 
achieving this is challenging with the current state of existing building structures. [22].  
In traditional window assessments, factors like energy efficiency and durability often take prece-
dence, neglecting the impact of materials [22]. Even with modern prefab windows designed for easy 
installation, challenges arise during removal due to features like flashing and finishes that may dam-
age materials [22]. Well-designed windows can be highly recyclable, but recovery ease varies by site, 
determined through onsite inspections and urban mining experiments [23]. Despite its recognized 
potential since 1976, fundamental principles promoting the reuse of components and enhancing du-
rability [24] are underutilized [1]. Evidence supports that disassembly significantly improves the re-
usability of building components [24]. However, widespread adoption of DfD is hindered by 
==PAGE:23==
Chapter 1. Background of the study  
 
9 
 
challenges related to cleanliness and waste management, primarily due to the lack of a standardized 
method for quantifying material’s reusability [24]. 
2.2.3    Incorporation of material banks and passports 
Urban Mining (UM) plays an important role in promoting sustainable construction practices by recy-
cling and reusing construction materials. This process involves the transformation of construction and 
demolition waste into new secondary construction materials or the recycling of materials from various 
components [1]. During the deconstruction phase, it is essential to evaluate materials carefully, as 
damaged ones may be deemed waste, rendering repair economically unfeasible [21]. Conditioning 
some materials to address issues like damage can render them suitable for future reuse or recycling 
[21].  
To enhance the transformation and evaluation process, one suggested approach is the implementation 
of a "material passport" system. This system would catalogue the properties of materials for future 
reference and utilization [1]. Quantifying the condition assessments of components and certifying 
them through a "material passport" is crucial for facilitating their reuse and recycling [21]. A material 
bank can serve as a platform for transferring materials and components, equipped with a "material 
passport," from deconstructed structures to new ones. This promotes efficient coordination through 
global planning, reasonable extraction, quality assessment, storage, and certification [21].  
Viewing existing buildings as "material banks" rather than "waste in waiting" is pivotal for increasing 
the utilization of reclaimed building components. In cases where immediate reuse or recycling is not 
feasible post-deconstruction, storage becomes necessary, highlighting the importance of implement-
ing storage strategies [21], hence measures toward storage strategies are necessary. The material bank 
plays a significant role in defining deconstruction strategies during the planning phase and may even 
directly participate in the deconstruction process [21]. Notably, Superuse Studios, a Dutch architec-
ture firm, has successfully implemented material banks, utilizing available materials within a 25 km 
radius of project sites, and creating visual maps of potential material streams for project design and 
communication [12]. 
 
2.3 The resource potential of reclaimed windows 
In this section, the resource potential of reclaimed windows is explored, emphasizing their significant 
role in influencing energy consumption within building envelopes. Central to evaluating their poten-
tial and energy efficiency are two critical properties: the U-value and the g-value. These metrics de-
termine the rate of heat transfer through a window, which can vary across its components such as the 
glass edge, frame, and centre. The U-value (W/m²K) represents the overall heat transfer rate through 
the window, while the g-value denotes the extent of solar radiation transmission, ranging from 0 
(opaque glass) to 1 (transparent glass). A low U-value is crucial for reducing heating energy con-
sumption, while the significance of the g-value varies depending on the climate of the building's 
location. While achieving a high U-value is typically straightforward with new products, maintaining 
it in installed windows poses challenges due to the durability and condition of window components. 
This underscores the importance of assessing and understanding these factors when considering the 
potential for reusing windows. 
==PAGE:24==
Chapter 1. Background of the study  
 
10 
 
2.3.1    Windows lifespan, durability, and environmental impact 
Exploring the elements of window construction (Figure 2) reveals 
that considerations such as lifespan, durability, and environmental 
impact are pivotal in assessing their suitability for reuse. A modern 
window consists of several elements, each playing a vital role in its 
functionality. The frame and sash of a window provide the structure 
supporting the insulated glazing unit (IGU). The IGU often involves 
two or three glass panes, with an interpane gap. The glazing is 
frequently coated with Low-E coating to allow sunlight while 
lowering solar radiation [25], [26]. A spacer separates the panes, 
typically made of aluminum or other materials. Gas, such as argon, 
xenon, and krypton, is used to ensure insulation between the panes 
[25]. The spacer is filled with a desiccant (often silica) to prevent 
condensation and absorb moisture between the panes [26]. Finally, 
gaskets are placed around the window to create a weatherproof seal, 
ensuring proper airtightness between the fixed frame and sash [25].  
The typical lifespan of a window is estimated to fall between 30 and 
40 years, with frames having a defined range of 30 to 50 years. Additionally, studies indicate that 
glazing typically has a lifespan of 20 years. In-depth studies offer varied estimations for different 
materials [27]. Aluminium frames are estimated to have a lifespan of 43.6 years in one study, while 
another suggests that aluminium-clad windows can last between 71 to 83 years. Regular maintenance 
can extend the lifespan of timber to 39.6 years, while other estimates range from 56 to 65 years. In 
contrast, PVC has the shortest lifespan due to frequent replacements for poor performance, approxi-
mately 18 to 24.1 years [27]. However, these estimates are all dependent on the durability, mainte-
nance, and restorations of the materials. 
 
Durability according to window type 
To predict the durability and service life of building materials, various analyses, and methodologies 
evaluate the assessment, one method is described in ISO 15686: 2011 [28], [29]. However, the method 
has limitations in identifying and quantifying durability factors, neglecting the degradation condition 
of the element under analysis, and overlooking the evolution of its degradation process over time. A 
study [28] proposes a new approach to address these limitations by integrating relevant factors and 
quantifying their impact on the degradation condition of the analysed element. The durability of win-
dows has a significant impact on their Life Cycle Cost (LCC) [28].  
The impacting factors on the prediction of the durability of windows differ among studies. While a 
significant percentage of thermal transmission loss can be attributed to windows, there remains a lack 
of research examining the impact of maintenance and refurbishment on window durability [30]. The 
longevity of window frames depends on various factors, including materials used and the application 
of coatings. For instance, coatings such as lacquer can significantly prolong the durability of alumin-
ium frames, while the anodic surface film outlasts traditional lacquering methods [28]. Timber 
Figure 2  A Guide to Window Detailing 
and Installation [66]. 
==PAGE:25==
Chapter 1. Background of the study  
 
11 
 
requires treatments to prevent UV damage and fungus, pigmented treatments have been shown to 
increase durability [28]. In comparison, the degradation of IGU is often associated with edge seal 
failures, breakdown of coatings, and inter panes gas loss [30], [31]. Double-glazed frames and suffi-
cient shading techniques are effective in increasing durability and improving thermal performance 
[28]. Studies suggest that gas concentration between windowpanes decreases over time, particularly 
when using 'regular' spacers like aluminium, leading to diminished insulation properties [30].  
Environmental factors, especially those near coastal areas (within 5 km of the sea), significantly affect 
window durability, with aluminium frames being particularly sensitive to degradation [28]. Further-
more, orientation influences durability, with aluminium frames more negatively affected by northern 
orientation (subjected to dampness due to wind and rain), while timber windows are more affected 
by southern and western orientation due to UV radiation and moisture [28], [32]. Proper maintenance 
and cleaning are key factors in ensuring overall durability, categorized into three scenarios [28]: 
• Scenario 1: Minimal intervention with maintenance kept to a bare minimum. 
• Scenario 2: Basic maintenance regime, emphasizing routine care and preventive measures. 
• Scenario 3: High-maintenance regime, involving more frequent and extensive interventions.  
Maintenance depends on the materials the window consists of; for example, timber alu-cladding and 
PVC frames may appear to require minimal maintenance (scenario 2), but periodic evaluation of their 
durability is essential [33]. To ensure the maintenance and lower energy consumption of buildings, it 
is essential to refurbish window frame assemblies, especially timber (scenario 3), and alu-cladding 
that allows refurbishment [34]. This not only extends the lifespan of materials but also positively 
impacts their life cycle, considering durability and recycling factors [35].  
Environmental impact of different windows life cycle phases 
Windows have a significant environ-
mental impact throughout their lifecy-
cle. Figure 3 illustrates the different life 
cycle stages a window undergoes. The 
lifecycle of windows starts with re-
source extraction, where raw materials 
undergo processes like milling, smelt-
ing, and melting. These materials are 
then assembled during the manufactur-
ing phase. Correct installation and 
maintenance play a vital role in ensur-
ing window longevity [25]. Eventually, 
windows reach the end of their lifecy-
cle due to thermal inefficiency or dem-
olition. While some of the window ma-
terials are downcycled or end up in landfills, others contain recycled content or are recyclable [25]. 
Figure 3 Window life cycle including reuse and recycling [25], [67], [68]. 
==PAGE:26==
Chapter 1. Background of the study  
 
12 
 
In the production phase, extracting raw materials for glass production, particularly sand and sodium, 
contributes to environmental impacts. The utilization of silica in processes such as heat toughening 
intensifies the ecological impact, accounting for approximately 60% of the total environmental im-
pact of a window [25]. During assembly, the choice of materials plays a crucial role, as both alumin-
ium and PVC windows have differing contributions to Global Warming (GWP), while timber shows 
a lower impact [25].  
The use phase significantly influences environmental impact, although the exact magnitude varies 
depending on factors such as building design and location, reflecting its complexity [27]. In the end-
of-life phase, recycling rates differ among the window materials. Container glass is often recycled, 
while post-consumer float glass and insulated glass units (IGU) commonly end up in landfills or are 
recycled to glass wool [20], [25], due to purity issues [36]. Timber and plastic frames face challenges 
in recycling, whereas aluminium boasts a 95% recovery rate [25]. Recycled aluminium requires only 
10% of the energy of virgin aluminium, enhancing sustainability [25]. For more detailed insights 
regarding the life cycle phases, refer to Appendix A.1. 
 
2.3.2    How to reuse or recycle windows based on industry practice and guidelines  
This section navigates the aspects of reusing windows within industry practices from two projects 
both in collaboration with the architect firm Lendager. The first project investigates insights from 
KAB's innovative approach and focuses on their inspiration catalogue and four-part guide. The sec-
ond from Miljøstyrelsen provides challenges and insights, regarding the reuse potential of secondary 
materials. Furthermore, it offers a checklist and how to secure quality assurance of windows (refer to 
Appendix A.5). A third guide [37] for the reuse of building materials made by VCØB can be found 
in Appendix A.3.  
Potentials for reuse made by KAB in collaboration with Lendager [38]. 
KAB has developed an inspiration catalogue, a four-part guide exploring the reuse and recycling of 
building materials. It delves into six materials suitable for reuse, analyses three specific construction 
cases, and introduces a dialog guide for circular material repurpose (see Appendix A.4). KAB has 
introduced four areas to qualify the potential of building materials: 
1. Environmental Qualification: Examining the environmental savings of circulating materials.  
2. Architectural Qualification: The circulation of materials introduces new aesthetics and iden-
tities in modern architecture. 
3. Building Technical Qualification: Assessing the strength and quality of materials through 
measurements and tests.  
4. Economic Qualification: Quantifying the economic value of reuse, considering logistics, 
cleaning, testing, and documentation.  
The four potentials cover all types of building materials to be reclaimed. When the potential of the 
window has been evaluated according to the four areas, the examination of the window can be made. 
Table 2 outlines an examination procedure for assessing the potential reuse and condition of windows. 
It covers various aspects, such as pane construction, condition evaluation based on scratches and 
==PAGE:27==
Chapter 1. Background of the study  
 
13 
 
potential cracks, window data analysis, remaining lifetime assessment through third-party testing, 
and considerations for the removal process. It investigates the presence of substances like PCB and 
heavy metals in window paint, and finally, storytelling. This approach provides a holistic view of the 
handling of windows.  
Table 2 Overview of how to evaluate the quality of glass from windows [38]. 
Examination 
Procedure 
Pane construction 
How far is approximately between the layers of glass? Is there a coating on the 
glass, and if so, what kind? What material is used in the spacer (aluminium, steel, 
plastic)? 
Condition 
An assessment is based on the number of scratches on the surface of the windows, 
potential cracks in the glass, and whether there is paint or anything else that needs 
to be removed. 
Window data 
In the window profile, the manufacturer may have written data about the glass's 
insulation values. Alternatively, the manufacturer can be contacted to inquire. 
Remaining lifetime 
Assessment based on third-party testing. In practice, it can be calculated assuming 
that the glass has a lifespan of 50 years minus the time it has been in use. 
Removal 
Evaluate how the windows can be removed. For example, in direct reuse, the win-
dows can be carefully taken out whole and placed on window frames standing 
against each other during transport and storage. 
Environmental screening 
Investigate whether there is PCB, heavy metals, or other unwanted substances in 
the paint on the windows. Generally, PCB is considered a problem in PVC 
frames. Depending on the age, the seal around the windows may also contain un-
wanted substances, and it is necessary to take a sample. 
Storytelling 
Do the windows tell a story, or is there something unique about the material that 
makes them worth preserving? 
 
UPCYCLE BUILDING MATERIALS - product, process & performance [39] 
The second project was made in 2017 when Miljøstyrelsen funded a collaboration with Lendager to 
develop upcycled products for Ørestad's Ressource-rækkerne and Upcycle Studios projects. The re-
port explores the circularity of the project, emphasizing the use of upcycled bricks, concrete, and 
windows in a puzzle design with a fixed frame to minimize construction site waste. Reusable win-
dows meeting energy efficiency standards are integrated into the building envelope. Windows not 
meeting criteria find use indoors or in external applications like sound barrier walls. The report out-
lines key parameters for evaluating material circulation potential in the context of circular construc-
tion practices, these are stated as follows: 
1. Cleanliness and condition: Circulation of building materials requires a certain level of clean-
liness. It is important to work with as high a purity of materials as possible to avoid being 
forced into downcycling. 
2. Quantity: The quantity of materials for circulation significantly impacts the final price per 
unit. It is, therefore, important to work with enough materials, as quantity has a considerable 
influence on the final unit price. 
==PAGE:28==
Chapter 1. Background of the study  
 
14 
 
3. Availability: In a construction process, the availability of necessary building materials is es-
sential. This involves both the timeliness of access to various material fractions and the pos-
sibility of harvesting materials in individual buildings. 
4. Quality: To succeed in upcycling building materials, the quality of the input material must 
align with the requirements for final performance (whether it is the strength of concrete or the 
performance of windows). This can be ensured by examining parameters such as age, strength, 
type, purity, and size of materials. 
To evaluate the circulation potential of windows, the checklist in Table 3, provides insights into the 
necessary inspections that collected windows and panes must undergo. This ensures their suitability 
for their use in the manufacturing of new windows with recycled panes. The high-quality windows 
in this project incorporated recycled glass with new timber frames and double-glazing. The manufac-
turing process includes the assembly of frame construction, sash, and multiple panes, sourced from 
demolitions, energy renovations, and production errors. Finally, after the inspection and the data col-
lection, the windows undergo detailed quality assurance, and testing procedures to ensure compliance 
with regulatory and project requirements. Refer to Appendix A.5 for a detailed overview of the quality 
assurance process.

and deemed acceptable.

V2: Beginning crack formation is usually classified.

V3: No "hole" in butyl is accepted. The sealing compound must not release 
the glass.

V4: Typically, 1, 2, or 3 layers of glass (Note, if applicable, insulating glass). 
Total thickness (V5) 
V5: Measure total thickness including all glass and cavities.

V8-10: Determine coating type with a flashlight, lighter, or device. Color dif-

often sun-shielding glass, and energy coating on side 3 (2-layer glass) or side 
5 (three-layer glass).

difficult to differentiate between stainless steel and aluminium. Plastic spacers

Dew point analysis (H1)  
H1: Check if the window is punctured with cold spray. Apply cold spray for 
approximately 10 seconds on the window from a distance of 5-10 cm. Wipe 
with alcohol. If visible condensation is present, the window is punctured and 
must be discarded.

H2: Extract dry matter from the spacer bar and weigh it before and after drying 
in the oven (destructive test). This test is only necessary for a few windows in 
a large series. 
 
 
2.4 Main barriers to window reuse  
Reuse and recycling strategies, guidelines, and examples of implementation exist, however, a sub-
stantial number of barriers hinder the widespread integration of reused materials. This section will 
investigate the primary barriers that influence the implementation of the reuse and recycling of build-
ing components in the construction industry.  
2.4.1    Technical, behavioural, and legislative barriers 
Barriers to the reuse and recycling of building components involve technical, legislative, and behav-
ioural aspects [36], [40]. Technical challenges involve complex and time-intensive processes, esca-
lating labour costs [1], [8], and reduced incentives for reuse and recycling. Legislative barriers relate 
to inadequate political priorities and frameworks for enhancing recirculation practices [8], while be-
havioural challenges arise from user preferences favouring new materials [36].  
Despite the acknowledgment of reusing or recycling building materials [1], the absence of specific 
legislative regulations for evaluating reclaimed materials remains an obstacle. This gap persists, even 
though it is recognised as resource-efficient and sustainable [1]. Companies also face challenges such 
as “End of Waste” criteria (criteria for the transition from waste to raw material) [41] and approving 
waste as recyclable materials. The responsibility for determining the approval lies within the munic-
ipality, and the application process can be complex, yielding varied responses [8]. 
2.4.2    Effectively assessment of reclaimed products 
Considering the evaluation of reclaimed products poses challenges, including issues related to docu-
mentation for load-bearing capacity, fire properties [42], and thermal performance [8], [36]. Win-
dows, in particular, present difficulties in reuse for new construction due to insufficient insulation 
properties, that do not meet modern requirements [42]. Although some windows find application in 
secondary construction, the environmental impact, especially from the glass, is a consideration. Re-
placing panes in windows has limited benefits, as the window's remaining life is expected to be shorter 
than a new window, which increases the emission of greenhouse gases per year [42]. Additionally, 
specific building components, like older doors and windows, may not meet current building regula-
tions for tightness and insulation [36].  
A streamlined analysis and evaluation process becomes imperative to lower costs and enhance the 
efficiency of the reuse and recycling processes [1]. Unlike new elements, which undergo sample au-
dits in industrial production to ensure consistent characteristics, this method proves unsuitable for 
reclaimed products [1]. Another obstacle related to the difficulty of universal application is the ab-
sence of CE marking [8], [36], or other quality criteria reducing the confidence in reclaimed products. 
It is often believed that building regulations demanding CE marking might hinder reuse. Challenges 
such as documenting a building product's performance, together with the lack of manufacturing 
==PAGE:30==
Chapter 1. Background of the study  
 
16 
 
responsibility and clear guidelines, make the responsibility difficult to assign [8]. However, introduc-
ing CE marking or similar labelling for reclaimed products would enhance trust and facilitate market 
access [36]. Effectively addressing these challenges is crucial for achieving a harmonious balance 
between maximizing building component reuse and repairing inconsistencies within the regulatory 
framework [1]. 
2.4.3    Price-competitive reused products 
Simultaneously, challenges persist in developing price-competitive products with reclaimed materi-
als, necessitating technological innovation and investments [16]. Reclaimed products are still related 
to higher costs compared to new products; thus, economic incitement remains a critical challenge [8]. 
The exploration of both the business and environmental benefits of material reuse remains largely 
underdeveloped, intensified by uncertainties regarding secondary material prices and associated 
costs, further complicating the assessment of these strategies [16]. However, material reuse demon-
strates significant potential for environmental impact reductions [43], varying according to materials 
and products [16]. The extent of these benefits is highly influenced by the recovery processes ensuring 
that the products fulfil the same functional requirements as their primary counterparts [16].  
2.4.4    Hazardous substances and the implications of window reuse 
The potential environmental benefits also involve consideration of hazardous materials within re-
claimed products. Certain building materials, whether reused or newly acquired, contain hazardous 
elements, ranging from PCB and asbestos to chlorinated paraffins and lead, and other hazardous sub-
stances [40]. These are carefully singled out during the demolition process to prevent any unwarranted 
spread and manage associated risks [40]. In addition, the presence of these substances also affects the 
working environment, handling, waste management, and potentially indoor climate during the use of 
the building. 
The reuse possibilities vary from window type and manufacturing year as outlined in Table 4. Double-
glazed windows may be directly reused, but caution is needed, especially for hazardous substances 
like PCB and SF6 during 1950-1977 [44]. Single glass panes pose no concerns for direct reuse. Test-
ing is necessary for substances in specific periods, particularly for double-glazed windows. Painted 
window frames, lead glass, and other materials need testing before safe handling. Components like 
edge sealing, paint, window kits, and elastic joints are non-reusable and non-recyclable, requiring 
careful handling and disposal [44].

Chapter 3. Methodology  
This section focuses on the methodology of evaluating reclaimed windows' condition and potential 
for reuse. The initial section (3.1) outlines the methodology and literature employed to conduct a case 
study on Life Cycle Assessment (LCA) and Life Cycle Costing (LCC) for various window scenarios. 
The second section (3.2) outlines the methodology employed to gather qualitative insights regarding 
sustainable reuse and recycling practices in the construction industry. It details the approach to con-
ducting interviews, collecting data, and categorizing information. The fourth section (3.4) presents a 
checklist for visually assessing the overall condition of five reclaimed roof windows. The fifth section 
outlines the methodology and standards utilized to evaluate and classify the air permeability of the 
reclaimed windows (3.5). The final section concentrates on thermal transmittance, detailing the meth-
ods and standards employed to assess the energy performance of reclaimed windows (3.6). Through 
these assessments, the aim is to develop guidance for individuals interested in extending the lifespan 
or exploring reuse options for reclaimed windows, contributing to sustainable construction practices. 
==PAGE:33==
Chapter 2. Methodology  
 
19 
 
3.1 The environmental and economic costs: a case study on windows 
This is a simplified additional study based on Life Cycle Assessment (LCA) and Life Cycle Cost 
(LCC) studies investigated in literature or executed in existing construction projects. The case study 
is based on four windows. The baseline comprises a new aluminium-clad window, with average val-
ues based on Danish windows (sourced from Vinduesindustrien) [45]. The other three studies encom-
pass various scenarios: the first scenario (Case no. 1) involves windows with 50% recycled glass 
content, combined with a new timber frame and sash [46]. The second study (Case no. 2) involves an 
old timber window paired with a storm window, which has been restored throughout its lifespan with-
out replacing the window itself [34]. The final study (Case no. 3) explores the addition of a storm 
window to an existing window [34]. Figure 4 illustrates the lifespan and description of the windows, 
impacting both LCC and LCA. 
 
Figure 4 Overview of lifespan and the different window assemblies and materials. 
Table 5 presents maintenance schedules that vary across different window types, serving as a refer-
ence for decisions regarding durability, restoration, and preservation.

3.1.1 Life cycle assessment of windows 
The comparative LCA includes the life cycles of three timber windows, and one timber alu-cladding 
window, all considered within the context of one window assembly. The functional unit varies in the 
different literature used; however, the declared unit for all is 1 m2. This specification ensures a com-
prehensive evaluation of the window's performance. The assessment relies upon the lifecycles ex-
plored in each literature investigated, ensuring that no assumptions are made to avoid inaccuracies in 
the environmental impact. It is important to note that the study applies a simplified approach, solely 
comparing results without delving into methodological specifics or allocation methods. The aim is to 
provide a clear and straightforward comparison of the environmental footprint of various window 
types throughout their lifespans. 
The literature utilized for this study is drawn from various sources, including Web of Science and 
DTU Findit. The literature forms the basis for the final results of the case study. The used literature 
varies; the baseline utilizes Environmental Product Declarations (EPDs) [45], while Case no. 1 is 
based on a report from Lendager [46], and the last two cases utilize a research paper. The cases exhibit 
variations in window types, encompassing side-hinged (baseline), casement (Case no. 2 and 3.), and 
a combination of multiple windows with diverse functionalities (Case no. 1). 
 
3.1.2 Life cycle cost of windows 
The four scenarios involve maintenance, restoration, and replacement over a 100-year lifespan. How-
ever, due to missing Life Cycle Cost information in Case no. 1, the analysis is limited to a 50-year 
lifespan. The functional unit remains consistent with the LCA analysis, with all windows evaluated 
at a size of 1 m2.  
Fundamental to this methodology are the distinct lifetimes for each window outlined in Table 6, which 
directly impact the frequency of required replacements and the overall costs over time. Supplemen-
tary factors, including environmental impacts and energy efficiency, can also be integrated, bearing 
significant long-term economic implications.

refurbishment, and replacement for each window type are outlined in Table 7. Notably, expenses for 
tasks such as cleaning and lubricating hinges and joints are not factored into the calculations. Like-
wise, costs associated with disposal are omitted, assuming uniformity across all windows. 
The included phases are materials, maintenance, and labour. The maintenance and replacement costs 
are dependent on the type of material. Table 7 outlines the maintenance, restoration, and replacement

3.2 Qualitative study: an investigation of industry practices 
The interviews are conducted with a semi-structured interview method employing an interview guide 
to gain a comprehensive overview of industry practices related to reuse, recycling, and general sus-
tainability. Structured conversations between the interviewer and respondents provide detailed in-
sights into companies' roles and current practices, shedding light on their perspectives toward material 
reuse and recycling. To ensure data validity, attention is given to internal validity, authentic and truth-
ful responses, and external validity, ensuring a comprehensive and representative information selec-
tion. All interviews were conducted online via Microsoft Teams, allowing for recording and transcrip-
tion by Microsoft Teams, however, a few were conducted through a phone conversation. Microsoft 
Teams meetings facilitated a focused approach to the respondents' answers. 
3.2.1 Stakeholder selection 
Stakeholders have been selected to provide a broad and representative overview of the potential and 
challenges across various phases of the construction process, particularly those influencing the reuse 
and recycling of building materials. In Figure 5, the construction process life cycle is illustrated, 
portraying the involved sub-phases, and aligning the interviewed stakeholders with their respective 
roles.  
 
Figure 5 The life cycle of the building construction process and the placement of stakeholders. 
The stakeholders selected for this study represent a wide range of knowledge in the construction 
industry. In the production and manufacturing phase, leading manufacturers like VELUX and Do-
vista, along with industry associations such as Vinduesindustrien and Træinformation, provide in-
sights into challenges and opportunities. In the construction phase, engineering consultancies like 
COWI and Søren Jensen offer perspectives on sustainable practices and challenges related to reusing 
building components.  
==PAGE:37==
Chapter 2. Methodology  
 
23 
 
In the use phase, housing associations like KAB, BOSJ, and construction company HusCompagniet 
contribute insights into building maintenance and refurbishment. For the end-of-service phase, spe-
cialists like Tscherning, focusing on disassembly and demolition, while Miljøstyrelsen offer insights 
into regulatory measures regarding the end of waste. This diverse range of stakeholders ensures a 
well-rounded examination of sustainability throughout various phases of the construction lifecycle.  
3.2.2 Interview guide and questions 
The interview guide concerning the stakeholders follows four topics, displayed in Figure 6, painting 
a image of the company's stance and engagement. The first topic i) explores the purpose and impact 
of the company in the construction sector; ii) uncovers the awareness and alignment with the existing 
norms and practices; iii) highlights the commitment to sustainable practices and its position on reus-
ing and recycling materials and recent or ongoing activities concerning this topic. Finally, the last 
topic iv) delves into challenges faced (personal experiences and contexts), exploring the obstacles 
and inconsistencies that hinder efficient reuse and recycling.  
 
Figure 6 Interview guide used to create questions for stakeholders. 
The questions in are organized according to the phase at which various stakeholders are positioned, 
as depicted in Figure 5. To gather comprehensive responses that allow respondents to elaborate on 
their perspectives, the majority of questions are formulated as open-ended. Table 8 provides examples 
of questions from various topics covered in all interviews; the rest of the questions asked can be found 
in Appendix A.8.

3.2.3 Methodology of analysis of qualitative data 
The analysis of the qualitative data has been a systematic ap-
proach aimed at extracting insights from respondents' inter-
view contributions. The approach to qualitative data analysis 
was adopted, following Creswell's methodology [48], [49] il-
lustrated in Figure 7. The analysis involved listening to inter-
views to capture nuances in respondents' responses accu-
rately. Subsequently, transcriptions were reviewed for preci-
sion and fidelity to the interview content. 
During analysis, significant sentences, and topics concerning 
the results were identified in each respondent's statements. 
These were linked to overarching themes and codes to organ-
ize and structure the data. Themes from each anecdote were 
compared to reveal patterns and similarities across the re-
spondent group. Basic themes, encompassing multiple anec-
dotes, were summarized to assess theoretical saturation and 
identify repeated statements. For a comprehensive overview, 
basic themes were further divided into keywords, highlighting 
prevailing themes and their interconnections (refer to Appen-
dix A.9). These keywords were then created into a code tree (containing keywords) to visually illus-
trate the most significant topics and their relationships among respondents. This test helped structure 
and present the results coherently for a meaningful interpretation of the qualitative data. Finally, ac-
cording to Creswell’s methodology, to conceptualize the results, the themes related to the research 
questions were reflected on.

[48], [49].

3.3 Thermal performance characteristics of reclaimed windows  
In the following Sections 3.4, 3.5, and 3.6, a series of tests will be conducted to evaluate the thermal 
performance of reclaimed roof windows, aiming to guide individuals interested in extending their 
lifespan or exploring reuse options. These tests encompass overall inspections to assess condition, 
evaluations of air permeability, and measurements of thermal transmittance. The objective is not only 
to determine thermal efficiency but also to identify any components that might complicate reuse. All 
tests are carried out at VELUX test facilities, utilizing their specialized equipment and data processing 
systems. 
3.3.1 Environmental conditions, origin location, and specifications 
Table 9 provides key parameters and environmental conditions at the original location of the re-
claimed windows. Assumptions are made about indoor conditions, such as a dry humidity setting and 
unknown temperatures. The details include the manufacturing year (2008) and a history of no regular 
maintenance, providing essential context before testing.  
The five reclaimed customized GGL U08 roof windows sourced from VELUX were extracted from 
the roof of a VELUX warehouse located in Østbirk, 8752 Denmark, through selective demolition. 
However, it is important to note that during demolition, the protective aluminium cladding was re-
moved, leaving the windows exposed to uncertain weather conditions for an unspecified duration. 
Two of the windows are equipped with smoke ventilation motors, while the remaining three are not. 
For a more detailed overview of which windows include a motor and which do not, refer to Appendix 
A.12.

Table 12 describes the testing parameters and methods employed in evaluating the reclaimed win-
dows. The air permeability test was conducted following DS/EN 1026:2016 [50] for the method and 
DS/EN 12207:2016 [51] for classification. Similarly, the thermal transmittance test adheres to DS/EN 
12567-2:2006 [52] for the method, with a supplementary classification based on the declared value, 
complying with the Danish BR18 requirements [53]. These tests aim to provide valuable insights into 
the condition and potential for reuse of reclaimed windows.

3.4 Overall condition of windows 
This section aims to assess the condition of the windows after 15 years of use as roof windows. 
Various methods exist to evaluate the durability and predict the lifespan of window frames, such as 
the application factor method [28], which considers specific factors described in Section 2.3. While 
this method is typically used for timber or aluminium frames, it may not directly apply to timber alu-
cladding windows. Nonetheless, it remains a valuable tool for assessing the durability of other frame 
materials. 
As detailed in Section 3.3, the cladding was removed during demolition, exposing the roof windows 
to outdoor weather conditions like rain and wind. Subsequently, a drying period was necessary to 
eliminate any accumulated humidity within the windows. This posed challenges in evaluating their 
condition post-deconstruction. However, a thorough evaluation of the current state of the windows 
was conducted, using the list below: 
1. Frame integrity: The structural integrity of the window frame was assessed, ensuring it re-
mained intact. 
2. Pane integrity: Examination was carried out to verify the state of the panes, confirming they 
were still whole and had no remarkable breakages. 
3. Leak detection: The presence of visible significant leaks within the frame or sash was scruti-
nized, aiming to identify potential vulnerabilities to external elements. 
4. Water damage: Any indications of water damage were examined to determine the window's 
resilience to moisture damage. 
5. Screw assessment: The condition of the screws was considered, ensuring they were both intact 
and appropriately attached. Attention was specifically given to screws connected to the sash 
and frame profile, scrutinizing whether they remained securely fastened or exhibited signs of 
loosening. 
6. Connection of panes and sash: The connection between panes and sash was closely inspected 
to verify its robustness and structural soundness. 
7. Verify the smoke ventilation window motor's operational integrity (only applicable for win-
dows with built-in motor). 
This approach to evaluating the windows’ condition provides an understanding of the durability, re-
silience to external factors, and overall suitability for its intended purpose.  
 
 
==PAGE:42==
Chapter 2. Methodology  
 
28 
 
3.5 Air permeability of reclaimed windows 
This section describes the air permeability testing procedure conducted on the roof windows, em-
ploying the DS/EN 1026:2016 [50] standard as the testing method and the DS/EN 12207:2016 [51] 
as the classification method. Moreover, the standard deviation was investigated to assess the discrep-
ancy among the results and to determine the feasibility of testing less than all the windows in a sample 
when evaluating air permeability. The standard deviation could have an impact on the possibility of 
developing standards for easier testing in the future.  
3.5.1 Experimental setup 
Figure 8 illustrates the setup used to secure the test window within the test chamber. It shows a) the 
test subject secured in the test plate, b) the test chamber itself, and c) a schematic representation of 
the fixed test window within the controlled environment of the test chamber. 
 
  
 
a) Pivot and opened window            b) Water and air permeability chamber       c) Concept sketch of secured test window 
Figure 8 Proposed configuration for securing the test window within the test chamber. 
The assumed roof pitch was 45° (Figure 8 a), simulating a gable roof configuration, it is acknowl-
edged that the actual pitch could be less than that in a warehouse. The temperature within the chamber 
was maintained at approximately 21.7 °C. Atmospheric pressure was measured at approx. 990 mbar 
as described in Table 13. In adherence to DS, the standard prescribes that windows should be opened 
at least once before testing. However, as the objective of the test is to evaluate the reclaimed windows' 
initial conditions, the window was kept closed during the initial testing (Figure 8 c), resulting in a 
deviation from the standard testing procedure. 
                       Table 13 Equipment specification

3.5.2 Experimental approach 
The specimens undergo varying pressures, ranging from 50 Pa to 600 Pa in increases of 50-150 Pa, 
according to DS/EN 1026:2016. Both negative and positive pressures are applied to the specimens. 
This pressure variation enables a comprehensive examination of how the air permeability of the win-
dows responds to different pressure levels. The testing process for the windows follows a systematic 
approach, as outlined in Figure 9.  
 
Figure 9 Overview of air permeability testing approach of all windows. 
==PAGE:44==
Chapter 2. Methodology  
 
30 
 
Initially, in test 1 the windows are tested as received without any alterations. Moving to test 2, the 
gaskets and windows are thoroughly cleaned, and the windows are subjected to three cycles of open-
ing and closing. Test 3 replicates the second test but includes tightening the frame profile screws to 
150 Nm and massaging the gaskets. Finally, test 4 evaluates air permeability in a single window, 
involving the use of the ventilation motor to open and close the window to assess its airtightness 
afterward. The detailed process, including the number of windows undergoing each test, is provided 
in Appendix A.11. 
The systematic methodology depicted in Figure 9, implemented across different tests, enables a thor-
ough assessment of air permeability. It offers valuable insights into window performance under var-
ying conditions and highlights the effects of maintenance. 
 
3.5.3 Experimental principles 
The airflow measurements of the test specimen indicated as Qx, are subjected to adjustments at each 
pressure test to determine the airflow (Q0) based on standard conditions outlined in DS/EN 1026:2016 
[50]. These standard conditions are characterized by a temperature (T0) of 293 K and an atmospheric 
pressure (p0) of 101.3 kPa. 
The calculation for airflow (Q0) is expressed by the formula [50]: 
𝑄0 = 𝑄𝑥∙ 
293
297+𝑇𝑥∙
𝑝𝑥
101.3 
 
(1)  
The parameters and variables are defined as follows:  
• Q0 is the airflow (m3/h) 
• Qx is the airflow measurements of the test specimen (m3/h) 
• Tx is the ambient temperature (°C) applied to the test chamber. 
• px is the atmospheric pressure (kPa) 
Moreover, the assessment of air permeability extends to the length of the opening joints and the over-
all area. Calculations can be performed to quantify air permeability in joints and the overall area, 
expressed in units of cubic meters per hour per meter (m³/(h · m)) for joints and cubic meters per hour 
per square meter (m³/(h · m²)) for the area [50].  
 
3.5.4 Classification according to DS/EN 12207:2016 [51] 
The classification method outlined in Table 14 categorizes the performance of the test specimens into 
distinct classes, ranging from 1 to 4, based on both negative and positive pressure measurements. 
Compliance with specific values is crucial, particularly concerning joint and area air flows, as these 
are essential for assessing the overall effectiveness and reliability of the tested components. The over-
all classification, which is an average of the classifications under negative and positive pressure, of-
fers a clear framework for evaluating and comparing the airflow characteristics of the test specimens 
==PAGE:45==
Chapter 2. Methodology  
 
31 
 
based on predetermined standards. Class 4 signifies the most favourable performance, while Class 1 
indicates a less desirable outcome.

3.5.5 Standard deviation of test results 
The standard deviation offers valuable insights into the variability among results, providing a clear 
understanding of the consistency or spread of data. By incorporating the standard deviation into the 
analysis, the findings become more robust and reliable, fostering confident and accurate assessments 
of the overall performance and qualities of the windows being studied. The goal is to utilize this 
method to evaluate a larger quantity of windows in the future. It aims to develop non-destructive 
testing techniques for a sample of window models sharing equivalent properties, manufacturing pro-
cesses, etc., rather than assessing every single window in the sample. 
During the experiment, data was gathered on air permeability by testing multiple roof windows at 
different pressure levels. The range of pressure tested was from 50 to 600 Pa. The standard deviation 
(SD) [54] was calculated for each pressure level across all tested windows. These calculations were 
applied to all five windows undergoing test one (described in Section 3.5.2). The SD represents the 
average deviation of each data point from the mean: 
𝑆𝐷= √∑
(𝑋𝑖−𝑋̅)2
𝑛
𝑖=1
𝑛−1
  
(2) 
Where; 
• Xi is each data point, 
• X̅ is the mean of the data points, 
• n is the number of data points.

3.6 Thermal transmittance of one reclaimed window 
The thermal transmittance is testing one roof window (window A12) in a hot box, following the 
standards outlined in DS/EN 12567-2:2006 [52] as the method, while the results have to comply with 
BR18 [53]. This method measures how efficiently the window retains or loses heat. The insights 
gained from this test are crucial for evaluating energy efficiency when utilizing a reclaimed roof win-
dow. Specifically, this test evaluates one window without any motor, which undergoes testing in a hot 
box for 24 hours until a steady state of conditions is reached, as per the standards [52].  
3.6.1 Specimen and mounting  
Before conducting the tests, steps were taken to address potential leaks during the installation of the 
window in the hotbox wall to ensure an accurate representation of the window's U-value, following 
the guidelines outlined in DS 12576-2:2006 [52]. The window underwent customization to fit the 
hotbox wall, with EPS panels (purple panels) utilized to fill any voids before installation, as depicted 
in Figure 11. Since the window initially lacked aluminium cladding, cladding from another window 
model was employed to ensure precision in the results. The installation process involved the strategic 
application of tape to seal any gaps or areas with insufficient aluminium coverage, thereby eliminating 
potential leaks. 
 
 
 
   a) Test window from inside with EPS      b) window with partly alu-cladding     c) window with alu-cladding and tape 
Figure 10 Mounting of test window for hotbox test. 
3.6.2 Experimental setup  
The experimental setup comprises essential components, as illustrated in Figure 10. This includes a 
box, metering (constant) chamber, cold box, specimen (installed in the hotbox at an angle of 90°, 
illustrated in Figure 10), heating and cooling system, and an array of sensors. The setup was designed 
to maintain constant temperature conditions during the experiment.  
Within this setup, a cold chamber features a fan (F) for temperature control below 10 °C, and a baffle 
with sensors (E) ensures a laminar flow between the cold chamber and the specimen. The metering 
box (A) was placed within a constant temperature chamber, featuring heaters (G) to achieve the 
==PAGE:47==
Chapter 2. Methodology  
 
33 
 
desired temperature, along with fans (B and H) to ensure uniform heat distribution. The specimen (D) 
was positioned between the cold chamber and the metering box. Numerous sensors are strategically 
placed in the hotbox, with nine sensors each on the baffles and specimen to measure airflow. 
 
Figure 11 Experimental setup of hotbox. 
In adherence to international standards, specifically, ISO 8990 [55] and DS 12576-2:2006 [52], pre-
cise parameters are crucial for reliable and comparable results. Table 15 outlines some of these pa-
rameters, including maintaining a temperature difference of 20 °C (tolerance margin ± 2°C) and 
achieving a steady-state temperature below 2°C in the cold chamber. This precision ensures the ex-
periment's reliability and accuracy. Regarding air velocity, a controlled upward airflow (F) of approx-
imately 2 m/s was maintained in the cold chamber, while the warm chamber experienced no down-
ward airflow (H). 
     Table 15 setting for experimental parameters.

3.6.3 Experimental principle 
To determine the U-value of the reclaimed window, heat flux and temperature differences across it 
were measured until a steady state was reached. The u-value can be calculated using the following 
equations [52]: 
Φ1 = Φp – Φ2 – Φ3   
(4) 
==PAGE:48==
Chapter 2. Methodology  
 
34 
 
R = A (Tsi – Tse) / Φ1 
(5) 
U = Φ1 / A (Tni – Tne) 
(6) 
The heat flow passing through the specimen, Φ1, is equal to the heat input required to maintain the 
metering chamber at a constant temperature, Φp, subtracted by the heat loss through the walls and 
surrounding panels, Φ2 and Φ3.  
 
Figure 12 Experimental principle of thermal transmittance. 
The parameters and variables in the given equations and illustrated in Figure 12 are defined according 
to the standard as follows [52]:  
• Φp represents the total heat generated (W) 
• Φ1 is the heat passing across the specimen (W) 
• Φ2 is the unbalanced heat or flanking heat loss measured parallel to the specimen (W) 
• Φ3 is the heat loss measured through the metering box to the constant temperature box (W)  
• R is the thermal resistance of the specimen (m²K/W) 
• A is the effective area of the experimental wall (m²) 
• Tsi is the interior surface temperature (hot side) in °C. 
• Tse is the exterior surface temperature (cold side) in °C. 
• Tni is the internal environmental temperature (hot side) in °C. 
• Tne is the external environmental temperature (cold side) in °C. 
• U is the thermal transmittance of the window (W/m2K).

35 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Chapter 4. Results of field studies 
This section delves into the assessment of the thermal performance of reclaimed roof windows, aim-
ing to guide individuals interested in extending their lifespan or exploring reuse options. It is orga-
nized into five segments presenting various research outcomes. The initial part illustrates findings 
from a simplified case study, examining the lifespan, environmental impact, and cost of four different 
window scenarios (4.1). The second study reveals results from an interview study exploring recycling 
approaches in the construction industry, capturing insights and experiences of stakeholders (4.2). The 
third study visually represents the overall condition of the windows subjected to tests (4.3). The fourth 
study specifically addresses air permeability, revealing complications arising from prolonged instal-
lation in a roof (4.4). Finally, the thermal transmittance, aims to unveil the energy efficiency of com-
ponents in reclaimed roof windows (4.5). These findings are imperative in shaping the development 
of a guide and facilitating more simple decision-making regarding the reuse and recycling of win-
dows.

Chapter 5. Results of field studies

4.1. The environmental and economic costs: a case study on windows 
 
This study is simplified, with most values and results drawn from the literature. The values from the 
LCA are derived from three different types of literature, whereas the results of the LCC primarily 
stem from assumptions and one literature source.  
4.1.1 Life cycle assessment of windows 
As outlined in Section 3.1, four scenarios were analysed. The initial scenario is the baseline repre-
senting an average of timber alu-cladding windows in Denmark. The second scenario is Case no. 1, 
utilizing 50 pct recycled windowpanes. Subsequently, Case no. 2, explores the scenario of maintain-
ing and restoring a storm window. Finally, Case no. 3, introduces the addition of a new storm window 
to the existing windows. Figures 13 and 14 present the results of the case study. The evaluation of 
environmental impacts across different window material scenarios reveals notable distinctions in im-
pact categories.
