4.1.2 Life cycle cost of windows 
The examined Life Cycle Cost analysis presented in Figure 15 provides valuable insights into the 
economic considerations associated with different window material scenarios over a 50-year lifespan. 
Examining material costs, the Baseline, representing an average timber alu-cladding window in Den-
mark, cost 3492 DKK. In contrast, Case no. 1, incorporating 50% recycled windowpanes, reflects a 
slight reduction in material costs at 3214 DKK. Both Case no. 2 and Case no. 3 suggest potential cost 
savings. The material cost of a new storm window is 2600 DKK showcased in Case 3., while Case 
no. 2 is refurbishing existing windows.

Labor costs vary across scenarios. The Baseline signifies the assembly cost of a new window with a

4.2. Qualitative study: an investigation of industry practices 
 
This section, examining the outcomes of the qualitative interview study unveils pivotal themes and 
challenges regarding the recycling and reusing of building materials in the construction industry. The 
discoveries offer valuable insights into the intricate landscape of sustainable construction practices, 
presenting both opportunities and considerations. The insights gathered not only provide valuable 
guidance but also serve to highlight areas where improvement is needed to promote the common 
adoption of recycling and reuse practices within the industry. These findings can help inform strate-
gies and initiatives aimed at fostering more sustainable construction practices. The most significant 
perspectives and themes are listed below: 
• Challenges in building material recycling: Recycling or reusing building materials involves 
identifying suitable materials, addressing uncertainties about hazardous substances, and man-
aging logistical hurdles such as transportation and storage. 
• Recycled material management: Efficient management practices optimize the handling of re-
used materials, including collection, sorting, transportation, and inventory management, con-
tributing to project sustainability. 
• Documentation and regulatory barriers: Clear documentation is essential for verifying recy-
cled materials' origin and quality, but inadequate practices and stringent regulations can hinder 
recycling efforts. Overcoming these challenges requires streamlining procedures and advo-
cating for supportive regulatory frameworks. 
• Shifting attitudes towards recycling: Educating stakeholders about the environmental benefits 
of recycling and promoting the aesthetic appeal and performance capabilities of recycled ma-
terials are crucial for fostering acceptance and adoption. 
• Balancing aesthetics, costs, and practicality: Recycled materials must meet aesthetic and func-
tional requirements while offering environmental benefits. Achieving this balance involves 
careful material selection, design integration, and cost-effective procurement. 
• Approaches to material reuse: Innovative technologies, collaborative models, and adaptable 
building designs promote material reuse. Transformative approaches include designing build-
ings for disassembly and implementing sharing and exchange initiatives. 
For a comprehensive overview presenting the code tree, themes, keywords, etc., please refer to Ap-
pendix A.9 and A.10.  
 
4.2.1 Stakeholder role and perspectives  
Table 16 provides a summary of the roles of various stakeholders involved in the discussion on recy-
cling and reusing building materials. For a more detailed overview of their perspectives, along with 
transcriptions, please refer to Appendix A.10 and A.16. This summary briefly captures the main per-
spectives and role that each stakeholder may hold regarding the recycling and reuse of building ma-
terials. Each company’s diverse statements and more detailed perspectives expressed will be thor-
oughly discussed in Section 5.1.

4.3 Overall condition of reclaimed windows 
The condition of the windows is assessed according to the checklist provided in Section 2.1. From 
evaluating frame and pane integrity to scrutinizing screws, leak detection, and water damage vulner-
ability. The focus is on providing a thorough understanding of the windows' overall condition, con-
sidering factors like the absence of visible issues such as fungus and the operational integrity of spe-
cialized features like smoke ventilation window motors.  
 
Figure 16 Condition of a roof window from different views stored outside without aluminium covering. 
Figure 16 shows a series of images of the condition of the windows after they have been stored outside 
without an aluminium covering. Below is an overall assessment of the condition of the windows:  
1. Frame and sash integrity: some windows display split frames (see Figure 16 image f. ) where 
they were fixed with screws, this might have occurred during demolition. The frames, how-
ever, remain intact but dirty, hence, maintaining a reasonable level of structural integrity. The 
==PAGE:56==
Chapter 5. Results of field studies  
 
42 
 
absence of visible issues like fungus in the frame and sash contributes positively to the overall 
window condition.  
2. Pane integrity: The panes have minor cosmetic marks, however, no visible remarkable 
scratches.  
3. Leak detection: Generally, visible leaks are spotted in each of the window’s corners. The gas-
kets work as they should and have no visible damage, other than that they have moved a bit 
away from their initial position (see Figure 16 image b. ).   
4. Water damage: A spot at an old screw hole on one window, induced by rain and seeping into 
the timber through the alu-cladding, highlights a specific instance of water damage (see Figure 
16 image g. ).  
5. Screw assessment: The screws remain intact, ensuring the proper attachment and stability of 
both the sash and frame profile. While some screws may have shifted slightly from their initial 
position due to weather changes and wear and tear, this can be remedied by tightening them 
back into place (the consequences of any such adjustments on the windows’ thermal perfor-
mance will be investigated in Section 4.4.). 
6. Connection of panes and sash: The panes remain connected to the perimeter of the sash with-
out visible separation. 
7. Verify the smoke ventilation window motor's operational integrity: The motor works fine and 
runs smoothly, confirming the operational integrity of the smoke ventilation window motor. 
In general, it can be seen that the condition of all the windows is satisfactory considering their age 
and that they have been stored inappropriately outside without cladding.

4.4 Air permeability of reclaimed window 
4.4.1  Detected leaks in frames, panes, and sashes during tests. 
To illustrate potential leaks under the worst-case scenario (constant pressure of 600 Pa), a series of 
images (Figures 17 to 19) depicting specimen A13 are presented. Smoke tests were conducted on all 
roof windows, yielding consistent results across the board. The frame sashes, a critical component, 
exhibit notable challenges, especially at the bottom and corners, displaying substantial displacements 
under negative pressure. Smoke tests reveal leaks between the outer perimeter of the sash and the 
pane under hight pressure, particularly evident in the sides with varying severity (illustrated in Figure 
17 image a.).

(interior side of the window).

top. However, sporadic issues are more prevalent along the sides of the window (see Figure 18).

The findings from the four tests demonstrate a notable enhancement achieved through minor mainte-
nance measures. Consequently, delving into the time consumption and complexity of each test pro-
vided valuable insights for individuals seeking to improve window performance.  
1. Test 1: involving the testing of the window in its original state (as received), was deemed 
insignificant for this analysis, and is not included.  
2. Test 2: involved a meticulous brushing and wiping process of the entire window and sealing 
strips required approximately 10 minutes.  
3. Test 3: involved tightening screws to 150 Nm, a task completed within a timeframe of about 
5 minutes. Notably, this test may face limitations due to the necessity of removing the alu-
minium cladding.  
4. Test 4, which entailed the mechanical opening and closing of the window, the process took 
around 10 minutes.  
These detailed results provide an understanding of the practical aspects associated with each test, 
setting the stage for further interpretation and discussion in subsequent sections.  
 
4.4.4  Standard deviation of results  
The standard deviation enables more informed decisions about the representativeness of the sample. 
A small standard deviation indicates that the results are closely clustered around the average, suggest-
ing a more uniform set of windows. Conversely, a larger standard deviation signifies greater variabil-
ity, and testing a smaller subset may not sufficiently capture the diverse characteristics present among 
all the windows. Tables 18 and 19 illustrate parameters such as the mean (average) air permeability, 
and standard deviation (SD). It assesses the consistency and variability within specific pressure ranges 
and analyse the spread of data points around the mean.

4.5 Thermal transmittance of one reclaimed window 
In this section, the assessment of the U-value result for a specific reclaimed window (A12) following 
the Danish regulation requirements [53] is discussed. A comparative analysis with the reference U-
value of two other windows for comprehensive insights is conducted. Additionally, the temperature 
change within the window is interpreted using thermographic images.  
4.5.1 Comparison between reference and new values 
The U-value is calculated according to the DS/EN 12567-2:2006, the U-value represents the heat 
transfer rate of the window and is calculated using the formula.  
𝑈=
𝑞
(𝑇𝑖−𝑇𝑒) =
1
𝑅𝑇 
(7) 
Where; 
• U is the U-value. 
• Ti and Te represent the indoor and outdoor temperatures, respectively. 
• q is the heat transfer rate. 
• RT is the total thermal resistance.  
The validity of the U-value measurement depends upon achieving stability in the system within the 
hotbox. Figure 24 displays a section (four hours) of the U-values derived from the Hot Box method 
[52]. The U-value measurements, ranging from 1.410 to 1.423 W/m²K, as indicated in Figure 24, 
confirm the stability achieved in the measurements. This stability is a key indicator of the reliability 
of the experimental data.  
 
Figure 24 Section of Uw variation over the measurement duration.  
The U-value measurements offer comprehensive insights into the thermal performance of the system. 
The calculated mean of the stability measurements in Figure 24 is approximately 1.41 W/m2K. Addi-
tionally, to evaluate compliance with Danish regulations, the solar radiation (g-value) value is im-
portant. However, this value was not tested; hence, reference values will be used for the assessment. 
The thermal transmittance (U-value) and solar energy transmittance (g-value) of the reference values 
are displayed in Appendix A.15. 
==PAGE:64==
Chapter 5. Results of field studies  
 
50 
 
4.5.2 Compliance with the Danish Regulation 
The Danish Regulations 2018 [53], necessitate an energy balance not falling below 10 kWh/m² per 
year for roof window and glass roofs. Thus, the energy performance of the roof window is calculated, 
involving calculations based on solar radiation, total solar energy transmittance (g-value), and the 
overall U-value of the window [53]. The calculation of skylights is performed for a tiltable reference 
window for a reference house with a roof pitch of 45° and is calculated using the following equation 
for roof windows  [53]: 
Eref = I x gw – G x Uw 
(8) 
Where;  
• I represent solar irradiation (345 kWh/m2 per year) adjusted according to the g-value's de-
pendence on the angle of incidence. 
• gw is the total solar energy transmittance (g-value) for the window at a roof pitch of 45°. 
• G is kilodegree hours in the heating season based on an indoor temperature of 20°C (90.36) 
• Uw is the overall U-value of the window.  
Applying the results from Section 4.5.1 and Table 11 the following energy balance is achieved: 
      E1ref = 345 · 0.56 – 90.36 · 1.41 = 66 kWh/m2 per year > 10 kWh/m2 per year        (9) 
E2ref = 345 · 0.54 – 90.36 · 1.41 = 59 kWh/m2 per year > 10 kWh/m2 per year 
(10) 
Substituting these values for the first reference g-value, Eref is calculated as 66 kWh/m2 per year, 
confirming compliance with the specified regulations. Additionally, a comparison of the second ref-
erence g-value, represented in equation 10, results in a reduced energy balance of 59 kWh/m2 per 
year. This implies a slightly lower energy balance compared to the first reference.  
 
4.5.3 Thermographic vision of temperature changes in window. 
The thermographic analysis conducted on various areas of the window provides invaluable insights 
into temperature distribution, revealing potential thermal bridges and variations in heat transfer. Un-
derstanding these thermal characteristics establishes a connection with the U-value, a key metric rep-
resenting the window's overall heat transfer efficiency. To highlight the temperature differences in the 
window, the surface temperature was measured using a thermographic camera in various areas of the 
window. Three images and their belonging graph show the temperature distribution for two bottom 
corners and one side flange. These areas were chosen since the corners and side flanges are areas that 
often are vulnerable to heat loss and common locations for air leakages impacting energy perfor-
mance.  
The average temperature for the double-glazed pane window derived from all images was calculated 
to be 17.4°C, respectively. Figure 25 to 27 illustrates a comprehensive overview of the temperature 
distribution across the window. Importantly, the figures also highlight the presence of thermal bridges, 
particularly noticeable in the corners at the bottom of the window frame and sash.  
==PAGE:65==
Chapter 5. Results of field studies

of the tested window. 
 
Figure 26 A thermographic image and its belonging temperature change graph for the left bottom corner of the tested 
window. 
 
 
Figure 27 A thermographic image and its belonging temperature change graph for one right flange of the tested window. 
As demonstrated by the data in Figure 25, there is considerable variation in temperature for L1, rang-
ing from 18.5°C to 12°C, with a difference of 6.5°C. Figure 26 reveals that L3 displays even greater 
variation, indicating a more pronounced thermal bridge, with temperatures fluctuating between 
19.1°C and 11.5°C, resulting in a difference of 8.1°C. Conversely, Figure 27 shows that while L4 was 
anticipated to exhibit a similar variation, it remains comparatively warmer, with temperatures ranging 
from a minimum of 13.8°C to a maximum of 20.5°C, indicating a difference of 6.7°C.

Chapter 5. Analysis 
This section critically examines findings from five distinct studies, conducting a comparative analysis 
across environmental impact, thermal performance, cost, and insights relevant to reuse and recycling 
within the construction industry. The initial subsection (5.1) delves into the environmental and eco-
nomic costs associated with various window scenarios. This includes an analysis of factors such as 
the use of virgin materials, recycling practices, maintenance requirements, and restoration efforts. 
Subsequently, (5.2) explores insights gathered through qualitative interviews. The third segment (5.3) 
focuses on evaluating the overall condition of the windows received for testing their thermal perfor-
mance, laying the groundwork for subsequent analyses. The discussion then delves into the results 
obtained from air permeability tests (5.4) and analyses their significance, particularly concerning the 
potential improvement of reclaimed windows through modifications. Finally, the section explores 
whether the thermal transmittance has diminished over the 15 years of usage (5.5). Through these 
analyses, this discussion aims to provide a comprehensive understanding of the implications of the 
findings and their relevance to sustainable construction practices. 
==PAGE:67==
Chapter 5.  Analysis of field studies  
 
53 
 
5.1 Analysis of the environmental and economic costs 
This section discusses the various window studies, with a focus on Life Cycle Assessment (LCA) and 
Life Cycle Cost (LCC). It aims to evaluate sustainable window options by analysing the relationship 
between environmental impact, thermal efficiency, and economic factors. Additionally, the section 
explores the implications of maintenance and restoration, along with the use of recycled and virgin 
materials. The comparison of assumptions, methodologies, and associated complexities in the re-
viewed studies will also be discussed. 
5.1.1 Life Cycle Assessment of four window scenarios 
The environmental evaluation presented in Figure 28 provides insights into the sustainability impli-
cations of different window scenarios.

tial, and Ozone Layer Depletion compared to the Baseline. This highlights the positive environmental 
impact of using recycled materials in window construction. Additionally, when considering thermal

performance alongside environmental impacts, Case no. 1 emerges as potentially offering both great 
thermal efficiency and environmental benefits. Properly selected and integrated recycled materials 
can provide comparable or enhanced thermal properties compared to virgin materials, contributing to 
improved energy efficiency. 
Although great results in several impact categories Case 1 exhibits a higher OD compared to the 
baseline. Analysing the heightened OD in Case no. 1 is challenging due to limited background infor-
mation and imprecise data available in the literature [27], [56]. Glazing manufacturing may signifi-
cantly contribute to this impact, potentially harming the ozone layer. Determining the exact cause of 
OD related to recycled materials requires thorough examination, but insufficient literature under-
scores the need for enhanced data availability in the project report [46]. Improvements are crucial for 
comprehensive analysis and targeted measures to minimize OD and other environmental impacts. 
Additionally, Figure 28 results are influenced by various factors, including different impact categories 
and modelling methods across scenarios, emphasizing the importance of standardized measurement 
for accurate comparisons. 
On the other hand, Case no. 2, emphasizing maintenance and restoration practices, also shows notable 
reductions in environmental impacts across most indicators compared to the Baseline. The reductions 
are generally significantly reduced than those observed in Case no. 1. Case no. 2 still presents envi-
ronmental benefits compared to installing entirely new components, as seen in Case no. 3. While 
maintenance practices in Case no. 2 help extend the lifespan of existing materials and may contribute 
to maintaining thermal performance, they may not achieve a good thermal insulation as it exhibits the 
worst U-values. 
Case no. 3, involving the installation of new components, presents mixed outcomes. While it exhibits 
reductions in some environmental indicators compared to the Baseline, it also shows increases or less 
favourable outcomes in others. For example, the Global Warming Potential increases compared to the 
other cases, suggesting potential environmental consequences associated with new installations. De-
spite the potential for new components to offer superior thermal performance, the overall environ-
mental impact may be higher due to the energy-intensive production processes and resource con-
sumption associated with manufacturing new materials.  
While each scenario maintains satisfactory thermal performance and mitigates environmental impacts 
compared to the baseline, Case no. 1 stands out for its emphasis on recycled materials, gaining the 
optimal balance between environmental sustainability and thermal efficiency. However, Case no. 2's 
reduced environmental impact underscores the significance of maintaining and restoring windows to 
extend their lifespan.  
Addressing the assumptions and limitations in these LCAs is a complex issue, given the varied scopes 
found in different literature and the diverse impacts of assumptions on durability and lifespan. 
Transport specifications in the assessments are inconsistent, raising questions about the reliability. 
Moreover, the varied methodologies (GaBi 2021.2, TRACI 2.1, and unknown) employed to calculate 
the results in the impact categories in the four studies further complicate the overall assessment and 
comparison of results.

Chapter 5.  Analysis of field studies

5.1.2 Life Cycle Cost of four window scenarios 
Figure 29 presents the variations comparing life cycle cost with thermal transmittance offering a com-
prehensive analysis of U-values. The LCC reveals relationships, with Baseline at 6,368 DKK and 
Case no. 1 experiencing a notable increase to 10,031 DKK. Case no. 2 outperforms others with di-
minished costs of 3,077 DKK, while Case no. 3 maintains a moderate value of 6,080 DKK.  
Despite Case no. 2 boasting the most favourable life cycle cost and an impressive lifespan, its thermal 
insulation ranks the lowest among all scenarios (Figure 29) [27]. Thus, while it may offer commend-
able longevity and advantageous cost over its lifetime, its potential difficulties in meeting energy 
efficiency standards must be considered [27]. The risk of significant heat loss through this solution 
could negatively impact its economic advantages during the use phase, particularly if increased heat-
ing expenses offset the benefits of a low life cycle cost after installation. Furthermore, the reliability 
of the lifespan depends on factors influencing the window's lifespan, such as exposure to wet and 
humid environments, and maintenance [33], [57]. This emphasizes the need to consider multiple fac-
tors when choosing window materials. While some windows may offer economic benefits and dura-
bility, potential energy efficiency issues may make other options more suitable, despite higher initial 
costs. Therefore, a comprehensive approach and careful evaluation of factors like energy efficiency 
and regulatory compliance are essential for informed decision-making regarding window materials. 
 
Figure 29 The life cycle cost vs. the thermal transmittance.  
There are several uncertainties associated with the LCC results. Prices for the Baseline, Case no. 2, 
and Case no. 3 are derived from the Sigma Browser Price Library, while Case no. 1, is sourced from 
a study by the Lendager Group in 2020 [46]. This introduces variations in maintenance, labour, and 
material cost. Lendager's findings for Case no. 1 suggest elevated labour and maintenance costs, pos-
sibly due to direct information acquisition from suppliers and contractors. However, unclear data 
collection and sources in Lendager's study create uncertainty when comparing results among the four 
cases. Furthermore, material prices fluctuate; Lendager's sourcing prices are from 2020, while the 
others are from 2023, highlighting the need to consider timing in calculations due to potential price 
changes over time.

5.2 Analysis of qualitative study  
The section addresses key themes such as standardization and legal support for quality documenta-
tion, overcoming recycling barriers, promoting transparency, balancing sustainability with material 
properties, reconciling economic realities with design, and regulatory role in promoting reuse. In the 
following, these aspects will be explored and discussed to gain a deeper understanding of the com-
plexities surrounding quality documentation and recycling within a sustainable framework. Through 
participants' perspectives and experiential insights, the aim is to identify key challenges, trends, and 
opportunities to inform future initiatives and improvements within this field. A comprehensive list of 
themes and statements along with potential sources of error can be found in Appendix A.9. 
5.2.1 Standardisation and legal support of quality documentation 
The growing popularity of reuse and recycling, coupled with heightened awareness of their environ-
mental benefits, has become increasingly evident. However, despite this positive trend, the practical-
ity of implementing these practices faces significant challenges. While there is a widespread acknowl-
edgment and acceptance of windows designed for optimal thermal performance, their potential for 
reuse is hindered by the current absence of comprehensive quality standards and legislative require-
ments governing reused and recycled materials. This limitation, as emphasized by KAB, highlights a 
critical point, as quoted: “The requirements have increased and increased and increased, regarding 
their thermal performance. And a window that is 5-10 years old may not be reused because they 
cannot comply with the new energy requirements” (Jesper B. Lindeberg, KAB). These challenges are 
further emphasized when it comes to documenting the quality of reclaimed materials to meet current 
legislative standards, complicating the transfer of reclaimed building materials between projects, as 
stated in the quote: "Once you transport building materials between projects, there are criteria for 
quality and documentation, creating a somewhat challenging situation" (Jesper B. Lindeberg, KAB). 
Recognizing the potential and barriers concerning reused materials, companies propose a compromise 
that involves demonstrating the enhanced qualities of reclaimed materials, thereby justifying a relax-
ation of existing requirements. Dovista, in particular, sees this as an opportunity to promote the wide-
spread reuse of building components, expressing the need to reconsider and potentially adjust current 
standards, as quoted: "We cannot continue with the standards and requirements we have right now, 
and somewhere, compromises may need to be made. So, one could imagine making some concessions 
somewhere and saying, 'If you can demonstrate that the recycled materials are better, we can ease 
the requirement's” (Heather L. Madsen, Dovista).  
In addition to establishing new standards for reused materials, Tscherning contributes to the discus-
sion by highlighting the perceived unfairness of existing regulatory models. While acknowledging 
the importance of focusing on requirements, Tscherning points out that these regulations primarily 
cater to new products, often sourced from virgin materials. This bias makes it extremely challenging 
for reused or refurbished materials to meet the stringent criteria set forth by current regulatory frame-
works, as quoted: “There are these regulatory models, and it's not that the requirements are unfair; 
they are, to some extent, sensible requirements. But there are two things about it, and it is the way the 
regulation is set up, focusing on new products” (Jakob Orbesen, Tscherning). These statements 
==PAGE:71==
Chapter 5.  Analysis of field studies  
 
57 
 
underscore the necessity for the establishment of new quality evaluation methods, standards, and 
regulations concerning reclaimed products, emphasized in the quote: "If there were standards, it could 
outline important parameters for recycling, whether it's a window or a brick, and suggest test methods 
to examine their quality."  
The discussion extends to the vital role of legislative support in the recycling process, reflecting a 
collective desire for a more supportive framework to enhance sustainable practices. Addressing a 
major challenge, Træ Information points out the current difficulty of complying with BR18 require-
ments when reusing materials. Træ Information states, "Our instructions do not specify whether they 
apply to new or old windows and must, of course, comply with the BR18 requirements, which can be 
challenging for used windows" (Mikael Koch, TræInfo). BOSJ and KAB suggest a potential approach 
for reusing windows if they do not comply with requirements. They both state that the windows can 
be reused in less demanding areas, such as staircases, emphasizing the importance of considering 
flexibility in requirements: "…, some windows could be reused in a staircase since there are less 
stringent technical requirements… we have implemented this kind of initiatives before in a project” 
(Ulrik E. Knuth, BOSJ). Additionally, KAB emphasizes the potential benefits of retaining some win-
dows in the building and repairing the others using spare parts, as quoted: “Could we replace half of 
them now and use the ones in the landings as spare parts for the discontinued windows with some 
remaining life?” (Jesper B. Lindeberg, KAB). These statements highlight that the current regulatory 
framework is designed for new products, revealing the challenges of applying existing standards to 
recycled materials. However, they also suggest that while implementing reclaimed building materials 
is feasible, it necessitates companies to innovate and facilitate the integration of these materials. 
COWI highlights that a significant portion, approximately 80-90%, of building materials have the 
potential for recycling, as quoted: "I reckon 80-90% of building materials have recycling potential, 
but unlocking this requires new processes, methods, and extending lifespan through warranties" (Emil 
Eriksen, COWI). These materials can be repurposed as interior components or refurbished to create 
new high-value products, reducing environmental impacts and reduce resource extraction. However, 
unlocking this potential necessitates the development of new processes, and methods, and the exten-
sion of lifespan through warranties. This facilitates the necessity of including new methods such as 
material passports to document and include important information about building materials. Addi-
tionally, they also highlight existing issues with current material banks, specifically noting the exclu-
sion of the reuse and recycling potential of reclaimed materials. This absence poses a challenge for 
buyers, making it challenging for them to assess whether the materials indeed have the potential for 
use in their projects, as quoted: "The problem is that those who manage the resource banks do not 
associate the same data with the materials. They lack the necessary information. You cannot see what 
the condition or potential is" (Emil Eriksen, COWI). On the other hand, Teknologisk Institut also 
raises a concern regarding the utilization of the material passports. While they appreciate the concept, 
they identify difficulties arising from the absence of essential information. This complication hinders 
the assessment of material condition and potential, despite the general recyclability of most building 
materials. They shared a critical perspective on material passports, pointing out challenges related to 
the time aspect, as quoted: "A challenge with the material passport is the time aspect, because if you 
tear down a house after 100 years, the house has been painted, maintained, and rebuilt many times. 
==PAGE:72==
Chapter 5.  Analysis of field studies  
 
58 
 
Many products with a material passport might be outdated and not updated" (Jakob T. Rørbech, 
Teknologisk Institut). These challenges highlight the necessity for comprehensive and adaptive stand-
ards to shape the future of recycling and reuse in the construction industry. A collective effort is re-
quired to establish frameworks that accommodate recycled materials, promoting a sustainable and 
circular construction approach. 
5.2.2 Disassembly, data gaps, and cultural shifts 
To overcome barriers, the industry must embrace new methods as previously mentioned. One method 
involves the ability to disassemble components. Currently, this presents a challenge as many buildings 
were constructed during a period when it was common to use adhesives and other materials that bind 
components together. Hence, prioritizing standards for the disassembly of reclaimed building mate-
rials is necessary, as quoted: "Establishing standards for the selection of separable materials pro-
motes sustainability and necessitates a learning process across the industry" (Emil Eriksen, COWI). 
Other consultants and demolishers also emphasize the practical challenges of disassembling compo-
nents and point out that reusing is often more expensive and time-consuming than opting for replace-
ment with new materials. While the need for careful time-consuming handling during disassembly is 
highlighted, they recommend colleagues follow specific methods and practices, as described in the 
following statement: “… We must guide on-site colleagues to follow specific procedures, methods, 
and practices during disassembly to preserve their value, preventing them from being discarded as 
mere glass and timber in containers.” (Jakob Orbesen, Tscherning). The perspectives highlight new 
standards in the choice of separable materials, and correct handling, generally reflect a commitment 
to sustainability.  
Moving towards sustainable practices, such as opting for deconstruction over demolition, symbolizes 
a fundamental shift in mindset, marking a paradigm change within the construction sector. One per-
spective focuses on the lack of a mature market, where consultants lack the training or education to 
incorporate recycled materials into their constructions seamlessly. Additionally, there is a lack of 
methods or standards for the easy implementation of reclaimed products. KAB specifically highlights 
the absence of a mature market that supports recycling initiatives, stating: “The consultants are not 
trained to use recycled materials. We do not have a mature market where advisers can just say, I can 
do this and that and then we can recycle. And that is even though a lot of time and money is spent on 
research.” (Jesper B. Lindeberg, KAB). Simultaneously, windows manufacturers such as Dovista 
acknowledge cultural transition as a challenge, highlighting the difficulties of reducing the cost of 
products while opting for sustainable solutions, as quoted: "Navigating a cultural shift and adopting 
a new mindset poses challenges, while the simultaneous goal is to minimize costs" ((Heather L. Mad-
sen, Dovista).  
Furthermore, as construction practices shift towards dismantling instead of traditional demolition 
methods, as a sign of a broader cultural shift towards sustainability, there is a growing demand for 
specific and updated standards. This is particularly necessary for handling materials containing haz-
ardous substances, particularly existing windows. The shift from conventional large machinery meth-
ods to a more meticulous process, involving individuals carefully dismantling reclaimed materials, 
necessitates an increased emphasis on safety in sustainable construction practices, as quoted: "... 
==PAGE:73==
Chapter 5.  Analysis of field studies  
 
59 
 
moving from heavy machinery to individuals ... This shift requires greater attention, with individuals 
delicately dismantling structures, necessitating heightened caution" (Olga Truelsen, Søren Jensen). 
5.2.3 Balancing economic realities and design considerations 
There is a complicated relationship between economic realities and the design and aesthetics of recy-
cled or reused products within the construction sector. Several perspectives appeared, illustrating sev-
eral challenges and opportunities faced by companies attempting to align financial constraints with 
environmentally friendly solutions. Builders express a commendable dedication to investing in sus-
tainability, showcasing their financial commitment to environmentally friendly practices. However, 
a nuanced discussion reveals a potential misalignment in economic incentives, particularly concern-
ing the willingness to pay. This raises essential questions about the economic landscape shaping sus-
tainability decisions. 
Other concerns brought up the potential for reusing components. Despite reclaimed products being a 
feasible and more environmentally friendly option they may not be the optimal economic solution. 
One potential issue with reclaimed products, such as windows, concerns the diminishing gas concen-
tration over time. It is emphasized that theoretically refilling windows is possible, however it is rarely 
economically feasible, as quoted: "It is also proven that the gas diminishes at some point in these 
windows. So, the idea of refilling them, theoretically, is possible. But economically, it may not be 
worthwhile" (Mikkel H. Larsen, Glaseksperten). This prompts a critical example of the economic 
implications of sustainable solutions, especially when faced with practical challenges. Another point 
is HusCompagniet's observation on waste management, emphasizing the relatively low cost of waste 
production compared to the expenses incurred in sorting it. This insight emphasizes a perceived lack 
of economic incentives for the adoption of comprehensive waste management practices. It has also 
become widely recognized that sustainable practices often come at a higher cost. This acknowledg-
ment highlights the importance of considering expenses when making decisions within this industry, 
particularly when working with a finite budget. The trade organization for window manufacturers, 
Vinduesindustrien, adds that recycling or reusing windows is more expensive than buying completely 
new ones: "Economically, it is somewhat more expensive" (Johnny H. Jensen & Elisabeth L. Nielsen, 
Vinduesindustrien). The additional cost of reclaimed windows may be due to the extra time and effort 
required to handle them, both during the demolition process and in preparing them for reuse. The 
assertion that new materials are almost free implies that associated costs, particularly those tied to 
craftsmen's work in Denmark, can become prohibitively expensive regarding reclaimed products, as 
quoted: “… there's an economic aspect to it. New building materials are nearly free, but when crafts-
men in Denmark are involved, costs tend to become excessive” (Jakob Orbesen, Tscherning). This 
perspective, encompassing financial constraints and the economic landscape, underlines the chal-
lenges encountered by companies as they strive to embrace more sustainable practices.  
The design and dimensions of reclaimed windows can indeed affect window prices and complicate 
their implementation. Since windows are typically ordered as made-to-order, integrating reclaimed 
windows into new constructions can be challenging due to the uncertainty of their sizes, as quoted: 
"Facade windows are largely made to measure and order, so it also imposes some limitations on 
recycling because you do not know the sizes in advance" (Jakob T. Rørbech, Teknologisk Institut). 
==PAGE:74==
Chapter 5.  Analysis of field studies  
 
60 
 
This shift poses a challenge for architects, as they must adapt their designs to accommodate prede-
fined window sizes, as quoted: "Architects must have an understanding of what there is and draw 
according to those dimensions" (Hector Grønborg, Flockstudio). To facilitate this shift, the use of a 
database could play a crucial role. By widely adopting databases or material banks, architects and 
builders would have access to comprehensive information about reclaimed components, including 
window sizes. Flockstudio adds, that such initiatives have been implemented before. The initiative 
was a development of a parametric software that could organize and tailor this data to the customer's 
needs. This software functioned as a parallel computing tool. A web-based interface made it easy for 
customers to input their specific requirements and receive customized solutions. Additionally, the 
acceptance of reused components' aesthetics is gaining traction in the building industry, although it is 
not yet fully embraced, as quoted “The aesthetics of recycling is not yet fully accepted, but it is gain-
ing traction with many builders” (Olga Truelsen, Søren Jensen). Finding a balance between aesthetics 
and economic viability is essential for the industry. Some companies are willing to compromise on 
aesthetics if they can achieve significant economic and environmental benefits, as quoted: "Of course, 
aesthetics is important, but I believe we can compromise on aesthetics if there are significant eco-
nomic and environmental benefits” (Ulrik Eggert Knuth, BOSJ).  
5.2.4 Improved transparency and overcoming challenges in recycling practices 
Ensuring transparency in sustainable recycling practices demands robust data integration and stand-
ardization, a critical aspect highlighted by industry stakeholders. COWI emphasizes the need for con-
sistent standards across the construction sector to foster efficient recycling, calling for collaboration 
in technology development, as quoted: “Data integration and standardization are crucial; it must be 
consistent internally and across the construction industry to promote transparency and sustainable 
recycling practices” (Emil Eriksen, COWI). The importance of data integration is widely recognized, 
but it is acknowledged that the technology in this area is not yet fully mature. As a result, industry 
stakeholders are encouraged to collaborate and work together to bridge this gap. One approach to 
address this issue could be developing technology that enables companies to share data and exchange 
insights on the utilization of reclaimed building products. This enables a collective mitigation of the 
existing gap and enhances the effectiveness of data integration efforts. 
The implementation of 'take-back' schemes is another concept that could improve the recyclability of 
windows and other building materials. However, this idea is not fully developed and widespread, 
some stakeholders express a desire for manufacturers to take back their products, potentially recycling 
or refurbishing them for resale. The concept presents logistical challenges safety considerations and 
other central issues regarding the availability and scale of materials. HusCompagniet addresses the 
level of assurance in terms of delivery when procuring reclaimed materials from suppliers to ensure 
that they receive the correct sizes, etc. at the required time, as quoted: “… It is of course also very 
important to us that there is a security of delivery. So that the window we have ordered has that size, 
that colour, or whatever it is, and so that the windows are delivered when we need them” (Elisabeth 
H. Wærsted, HusCompagniet). This suggests that comprehensive measures have to be implemented 
throughout the supply chain to guarantee that buyers receive the necessary products at the right time. 
==PAGE:75==
Chapter 5.  Analysis of field studies  
 
61 
 
The aim is to ensure that the purchasing process for reclaimed products adheres to the same standards 
as buying new ones. For buyers to have confidence in the quality and reliability of reclaimed products. 
The challenge of ensuring the quality and reliability of reclaimed products extends beyond the con-
struction site to housing companies. There is a general concern among buyers about purchasing re-
claimed products, which highlights the need for guarantees and warranties for recycled materials. 
“We often have warranties that ensure our customers, and concerning recycled windows, they would 
need to have the same guarantees. We would not be happy to deliver something that could lead to 
complaints” (Elisabeth H. Wærsted, HusCompagniet). However, companies may be more inclined to 
implement such solutions if they are managed by reputable companies. Overall, stakeholders express 
significant uncertainty and opposition regarding the responsibility for materials quality assurance. 
Teknologisk Institut raises questions about the complex relationship of responsibilities among stake-
holders, particularly for manufacturers selling products across Europe.  
The utilization of reclaimed products in construction projects can be complicated, especially when 
dealing with a large number of windows. Managing the movement of reclaimed windows from vari-
ous projects requires a systematic approach to handle the scale effectively. However, a significant 
challenge arises in the subsequent take-back and reuse process, particularly in the logistical aspect of 
storage. Storing windows post-deconstruction becomes difficult, especially when considering the 
need to store them for refurbishment before resale. Additionally, the lack of suitable technology for 
proper storage further increases the risk of resource loss if materials are discarded due to inadequate 
storage solutions, as quoted: “While the technology might not be ready now, there is potential within 
the next 5-10 years. Discarding everything currently due to uncertainty, risks a significant loss of 
resources, highlighting the current lack of suitable storage options” (Emil Eriksen, COWI). This 
highlights the importance of addressing these storage challenges to prevent unnecessary waste and 
maximize the potential reuse of reclaimed materials. 
5.2.5 The relationship between regulation and sustainable practices  
The intricate relationship between regulations and reuse practices is a critical aspect highlighted by 
Miljøstyrelsen, emphasizing that materials removed from buildings are often immediately labelled as 
waste, placing materials at the bottom of the Waste Hierarchy (Section 2.1). This challenge is shared 
by stakeholders such as BOSJ and KAB, who recognize the complexity it introduces to the recycling 
process. Responding to the demand for stricter regulations from various stakeholders, Miljøstyrelsen 
reports initiatives to be implemented in legislation, with a specific focus on selective demolition. 
However, it is noteworthy that these measures are proposed to apply only to buildings with floors 
larger than 250 square meters. A decisive element of these upcoming regulations is the introduction 
of a resource screening requirement for buildings. This test is designed to seamlessly integrate reused 
materials by ensuring they are properly treated before being reclassified as products. The emphasis 
on resource screening aligns with the broader concept of "End of Waste," a key player in the reuse 
concept. The idea is that building materials should undergo thorough analysis rather than being auto-
matically labelled as waste. This shift in perspective toward a more nuanced and comprehensive ap-
proach contributes to fostering a more environmentally conscious demolition industry. 
==PAGE:76==
Chapter 5.  Analysis of field studies  
 
62 
 
Furthermore, the regulatory framework introduces an environmental screening process to confirm 
that recycled materials meet stringent standards and regulations. This strategic regulatory approach 
plays a pivotal role in encouraging recycling efforts within the construction sector. By creating a 
framework that not only discourages the automatic categorization of materials as waste but also ac-
tively promotes resource screening and adherence to environmental standards, regulators are contrib-
uting to the development of a sustainable and environmentally friendly industry. 
The selective application of these regulations to larger buildings reflects a targeted approach, ac-
knowledging the diverse challenges faced by different scales of construction projects. The focus on 
selective demolition aligns with the industry's shift toward more sustainable practices, highlighting 
the role regulations play in shaping behaviours and practices within the construction and demolition 
sectors. As these regulations are implemented, they are expected to contribute significantly to the 
promotion of reuse practices, setting a precedent for environmentally conscious approaches in the 
broader construction industry.

5.3 Overall condition of reclaimed windows 
The evaluation of the overall condition of the windows, detailed in Section 3.4, provides valuable 
insights into their structural soundness and operational functionality. Despite critical storage condi-
tions, the windows display resilience, presenting a satisfactory state that reflects their ability to with-
stand the challenges of aging and prolonged weather exposure.  
Several important features are discussed concerning the overall visual inspection of windows, which 
can affect their thermal performance. Visually, the panes and their structural and thermal performance 
appear adequate, showing no cracks or visible flaws, and are properly connected to the sash perimeter. 
The examination of the windows has shown that the gaskets (Figure 30) have maintained a reasonable 
condition without displaying any signs of deterioration.  
  
 
Figure 30 The condition of the gasket showing no signs of deterioration (left), and exposed crack in the wooden frame (right). 
The integrity of the gaskets plays a significant role in preventing leaks and ensuring the overall in-
tegrity of windows. A study [57] demonstrated that these gaskets effectively prevented leaks, even 
when subjected to challenging conditions such as fluctuating solar radiation and temperatures. The 
study further supports the resilience of rubber gaskets in maintaining their functionality and reliability 
over time [57]. These outcomes emphasize the critical role of gaskets in preserving the functionality 
and longevity of windows, a factor also evaluated in the air permeability testing section (Section 4.4). 
Regarding the timber frame as seen in Figure 30, there is a section where the wood has become 
exposed. This may be due to the removal of cladding or be the result of exposure to moisture and 
temperature [58], [59]. Studies indicate that improperly treated wood surfaces can be affected by high 
humidity and temperature variations [33], [58]. This highlights the complexity of determining 
whether the condition of the frame and sash is a result of gradual degradation over the years or a 
consequence of exposure to various weather conditions without its alu-cladding. The lack of infor-
mation regarding the condition of cladding and the intricate effects of weather conditions highlights 
the need for further investigations and documentation to obtain a more accurate assessment of the 
windows' condition and any mechanisms of deterioration.

5.4 Analysis of air permeability of reclaimed windows 
The air permeability tests conducted on reclaimed roof windows provided valuable insights into their 
performance and areas for potential improvement under specific conditions. Examining the observed 
results involves considering various factors influencing air permeability and the implications of any 
modifications made. 
5.4.1 The reclaimed windows' performance under pressure 
The investigation into the air permeability of reclaimed windows revealed significant findings, high-
lighting both challenges and opportunities for enhancement. Notable difficulties were observed under 
high-pressure conditions, particularly at the bottom and corners of the frame sashes. Smoke tests 
further identified concentrated leaks between the glass and sash perimeter and the corners, emphasiz-
ing vulnerabilities in specific areas (Refer to Figures 17 to 19). These areas require special attention 
when considering window reuse, as leaks in these areas can significantly impact the windows' air-
tightness and may necessitate additional measures before they are suitable for reuse.  
Analysed results for windows tested in their received condition (Figure 31) showed that all windows 
remained within Class A3 according to DS/EN 12207:2016 [51] for positive pressure, with one win-
dow meeting Class A4 standards. A closer examination of the negative pressure results revealed that 
the air permeability in the windows resulted in an overall classification of poor quality. More pre-
cisely, the results only met Class 2 standards, with one meeting Class 3 standards according to DS/EN 
12207:2016 [51].  
 
 
Figure 31 Comparison of overall classification (area and joint) across negative and positive pressure of the reclaimed  
windows subjected to the first test (tested as received).  
It became apparent that negative pressure induced a suction effect on the windows, causing the glass 
and frames to be pulled inward. This adversely affected air permeability negatively (Figure 31). This 
trend applied to both areas and joints, although joints displayed poorer performance compared to 
areas (see Figure 21). This discrepancy may stem from environmental factors such as moisture, tem-
perature fluctuations, and mechanical stresses, which can gradually degrade the joints over time [60], 
potentially compromising their ability to maintain air tightness and structural integrity. The disparity 
may also be attributed to the variance between the measurements of joints, expressed in meters

(m3/hm) along the sash perimeter, and the area measurement, which encompasses the entirety of the 
window area (m3/hm2). However, notably poor outcomes were particularly prominent during high 
pressure above 300 Pa (see Figure 21). The negative impact of high pressure on the windows can be 
attributed to factors such as distortion or deformation in window materials, particularly if they are not 
designed to withstand significant suction forces, such as 600 Pa. Other reasons may include pressure-
induced seal compression, movement, gasket discontinuity, or sash fit, creating gaps or openings, 
allowing air to enter the window assembly [60]. A pressure-induced seal compression phenomenon 
is more likely to occur under negative pressure conditions, leading to a greater increase in air leakage 
compared to positive pressure. In real-world scenarios, windows are subjected to varying external 
pressure conditions due to factors such as wind speed and building orientation. While the results 
suggest higher air permeability, particularly after 300 Pa, it is noteworthy that Denmark rarely expe-
riences wind pressures exceeding 600 Pa under normal weather conditions [61]. However, pressures 
reaching 900-1000 Pa during extreme weather events could occur occasionally [61]. 
 
5.4.2 Impact of alterations and maintenance on the air tightness of reclaimed windows 
The presented data showcases the air permeability results regarding the four tests conducted on the 
roof windows. Test 1 represents the initial performances for the received used windows, while Test 2 
involves thorough cleaning of the windows both inside and outside, with a particular focus on the 
gaskets. Test 3 tightens the frame screws to 150 Nm and massages the gaskets, while test 4 tests the 
air permeability when the windows have been opened and closed a couple of times with a mechanical 
smoke motor. 
 
Figure 32 Classification of alterations of reclaimed windows compared to the reference CE marking air tightness. 
The results depicted in Figure 32 reveal a noticeable trend towards enhanced classification with each 
alteration applied to the windows. Initially, at Alteration 1, windows were categorized as A2 and A3, 
indicating moderate to low levels of air permeability. Subsequently, in Alteration 2, windows A12 
and A13 increased in classification from A2 to A3, while A09 retained its initial Class 3. In Alteration 
3, A09 increased to Class A4, while A13 remained in Class A3. The consistent shift in classification 
across all windows signifies a notable improvement in airtightness. Alteration 4 yielded identical

classifications, indicating no obvious vulnerabilities associated with mechanical ventilation and its 
impact on window airtightness. The variations in alterations and resulting classifications among the 
windows underscore the diverse vulnerabilities inherent to each reclaimed roof window. Comparing 
these results to the reference window, which had a classification of A3, highlights the impressive 
effectiveness of the maintenance measures implemented. The progressive improvement observed 
with each alteration underscores the impact of targeted maintenance, such as cleaning, tightening 
frame screws, and massaging gaskets, in enhancing the thermal performance and airtightness of the 
windows. 
To assess the feasibility and level of complexity of these actions for homeowners, the time and effort 
required to perform the first three alterations were considered. The first test was not included as it 
represents an alteration that may not necessarily contribute to improving the window. In total, it took 
approximately 25 minutes to conduct these maintenance measures. It is important to acknowledge 
that Test 3 entails the removal of cladding and the tightening of these screws, a task that may best be 
performed by a professional.  The benefits of enhancements weighed against the time invested in each 
test underscore the potential for reclaimed windows to achieve superior airtightness with minimal 
adjustments. Even simple measures like cleaning the window and massaging the gaskets can lead to 
an overall improvement in the airtightness classification. The favourable impact of these modifica-
tions, especially the tightening of frame screws, suggests that focused adjustments can successfully 
address specific vulnerabilities. 
 
5.4.3 The standard deviation of air permeability of reclaimed windows as received 
The results of the five tested roof windows, as presented in Figure 33, demonstrate variations in the 
standard deviation (SD) of air permeability measurements at different pressure levels. At lower pres-
sure levels, such as 50 and 300 Pa, moderate SD values are observed both for the total window area 
and for the joints, indicating a relatively consistent performance among the tested windows at these 
levels. However, as the pressure levels increase to 450 and 600 Pa, significantly higher SD values are 
observed for both the window area and the joints.

The observed variability among the tested windows in both joints and areas could stem from differ-
ences in mechanical components, wear and tear over time, and variations in placement within the roof 
structure, leading to varying exposure to wind and solar radiation [35]. Additionally, environmental 
exposure [33] during storage, such as being positioned at the top of a stack outdoors, could also play 
a role in influencing the performance of individual windows [59]. 
To understand the magnitude of the SD values and assess the variability more accurately, the coeffi-
cient of variation (CV) [62] was calculated alongside the standard deviation. Incorporating the coef-
ficient of variation alongside SD provides a more robust assessment of variability, particularly in 
situations where the magnitude of data differs significantly. The coefficient of variation, symbolized 
as CV, serves to normalize the standard deviation of a series of numbers by dividing it by the mean 
of the sample. Formally, for a series of N numbers with standard deviation S and mean M, the coef-
ficient of variation [62] is calculated as: 
𝐶𝑉=
𝑆
𝑀           (11) 
The observed coefficient of variation (CV) appears to be relatively low considering the sample size 
tested, suggesting a variation of approximately 0.3 of the SD relative to the mean values of the sam-
ples. A general rule of thumb is that a CV value less than 1 can be categorized as indicating low 
variance, which suggests minimal variability among the SD results. This observation holds signifi-
cance, particularly given the small sample size of only five windows. Highlighting the need for addi-
tional research to refine testing efforts for assessing the air permeability of reclaimed windows. 
While the current study provides valuable insights based on limited sample size, future research 
should validate and confirm these findings using larger samples to ensure robustness. Efforts should 
be directed toward developing new testing methods for assessing window performance without the 
need to test every window within the sample. By strategically selecting windows based on criteria 
such as age, condition, and material properties, testing costs and resource expenditures can be mini-
mized while ensuring reliable results with a representative subgroup of the windows. This approach 
enables the identification of key performance indicators and outliers within the window sample, fa-
cilitating informed decision-making regarding refurbishment, retrofitting, or replacement priorities. 
Utilizing statistical techniques like stratified sampling methodologies ensures the representativeness 
and validity of the tested samples while optimizing resource allocation and time efficiency. Balancing 
comprehensive data collection with practical considerations of cost-effectiveness maximizes the util-
ity of available resources in developing and implementing new testing methods for reclaimed prod-
ucts.  
5.4.4 Sources of error 
This section explores the sources of error, shedding light on their implications for air permeability 
testing and the broader understanding of reclaimed window performance. In assessing the air perme-
ability of reclaimed windows, it is important to acknowledge potential sources of error that may affect 
the reliability and accuracy of test results. Variability among windows, mechanical influences, and 
post-demolition conditions pose significant challenges in interpreting test outcomes.  
==PAGE:82==
Chapter 5.  Analysis of field studies  
 
68 
 
The observed enhancements resulting from minor refurbishments may not apply universally to all 
reclaimed windows, due to variations in conditions and material states. Secondly, the condition of 
reclaimed windows obtained after demolition poses a potential source of error in the air permeability 
testing process. The windows lacked cladding and were exposed to various weather conditions during 
their post-demolition state without any protective covering, introducing uncertainties in the testing 
results. Exposure to weather elements such as rain, wind, and fluctuating temperatures could lead to 
changes in the integrity of the windows [28], [33], [59]. Expansion, contraction, and other environ-
mental impacts might influence the overall airtightness and performance of the reclaimed windows 
[59]. As a result, the condition of these windows at the time of testing may not accurately represent 
their original performance characteristics. This source of error emphasizes the importance of ac-
knowledging the potential variability in the condition of reclaimed windows and considering it in the 
interpretation of air permeability test results. While efforts can be made to refurbish or address po-
tential issues, it remains a challenge to fully eliminate the impact of weather exposure during the 
period between demolition and testing.

5.5 Analysis of thermal transmittance of one reclaimed window 
This section assesses the results and associated uncertainties regarding the findings. The comparison 
between the tested thermal transmittance and the reference values offers valuable insights into the 
thermal performance of the window assembly and any potential changes that may have occurred dur-
ing its installation in the roof. 
Firstly, the tested thermal transmittance of 1.41 W/m²K falls within a narrow range between the two 
reference values: 1.4 W/m²K and 1.5 W/m²K, as illustrated in Figure 34. This indicates that the meas-
ured thermal transmittance is close to both reference values, suggesting a relatively accurate assess-
ment of the window's thermal conductivity. The slight deviation of 0.01 W/m²K from the first refer-
ence value of 1.4 W/m²K is minimal and falls within an acceptable margin of error. Similarly, the 
deviation of 0.09 W/m²K from the second reference value of 1.5 W/m²K, while slightly larger, re-
mains small. 
 
Figure 34 Comparison of u-value in the reference and tested/reclaimed window. 
These results suggest that the reclaimed roof window assembly exhibits thermal performance com-
parable to the reference windows, with only minor differences observed. This is especially notewor-
thy considering the crucial role of thermal transmittance in defining a window's energy efficiency and 
overall thermal performance following its use. 
5.5.1 The impact of U- and g-values on a reclaimed windows energy balance 
The analysis of energy balance for the first reference windows revealed an energy balance of 66 
kWh/m² per year, while the second reference g-value had a reduced value of 59 kWh/m² per year, 
slightly lower than that of the first reference, as seen in Figure 35. This complies with the regulatory 
threshold of 10 kWh/m² per year [53], however, the discrepancy between the energy balance values 
for the reference g-values suggests a sensitivity to changes in the g-value.  
The sensitivity of the g-value is further addressed. The g-values used in the Eref calculations are de-
rived from the reference windows and not from the tested reclaimed window. Consequently, while

the current calculations using reference g-values indicate compliance with BR18 [53], the potential 
unknown changes in the g-value and the reclaimed window’s g-value could affect compliance [31]. 
Discrepancies with the presence of an unknown type of white film on the tested window could intro-
duce potential errors in g-value assessments. The film can mitigate solar radiation entering through 
the window by reflecting or absorbing a portion of the sun's energy. Studies suggest a correlation 
between window film and the g-value in windows, indicating that the film's presence could potentially 
alter the g-value [63], [64]. Despite similarities in the glass structure among the reference windows 
and the reclaimed, assessing the window's g-value is necessary for further investigation. 
 
Figure 35 g-value vs. energy balance.  
Uncertainties arise regarding the practical applicability of U-value results utilized in calculating the 
Eref. Primarily, there is uncertainty regarding the measurement method; the U-value was assessed in 
a hotbox at a 90° angle, whereas the standard reference value, Eref [53], for roof windows is typically 
calculated at a 45° angle. At lower angles, the IGU will experience higher U-values [65]. This phe-
nomenon stems from the turbulent flow exhibited by the gas under these conditions. Conversely, with 
increasing angles, the flow transitions toward a more stable laminar state [65]. Hence, the derived U-
value from testing at a 90° angle could potentially yield a more favourable U-value compared to the 
actual value, as window thermal performance typically excels at a 90° angle compared to a 45° angle. 
This suggests that the true U-value of the measured roof window may indeed be higher than 1.41 
W/m²K, as measured in this study. 
 
5.5.2 Thermal bridges and the influence on window performance 
Thermal bridges identified through thermographic vision signify potential inefficiencies in heat trans-
fer within the window assembly (Figures 25 to 27). These bridges may result from negative pressure, 
loosening of frame screws, compromised weather seals, or displacement between the frame and sash. 
Clear indications of thermal bridges, particularly at the sash perimeter and corners, suggest areas of 
less efficient heat transfer, leading to potential energy losses. Understanding the significance of ther-
mal bridges is crucial for optimizing overall thermal efficiency. These areas signify reduced thermal

resistance, which facilitates heat transfer and potentially leads to increased energy consumption. Ne-
cessitating the need for remedial action to enhance insulation and reduce heat transfer. 
Moreover, thermal bridges can affect the calculation of the U-value. Higher thermal bridging leads to 
an increased U-value, signifying reduced insulation performance and higher energy consumption. To 
enhance the thermal performance of windows and mitigate thermal bridges, various strategies can be 
employed. These include enhancing insulation and sealing around the window frame, sash, and cor-
ners, undertaking comprehensive energy retrofits, and conducting regular maintenance to address 
sealing issues and ensure optimal thermal performance. 
 
5.5.3 Uncertainties associated with the reference U-values and test methods. 
There are several uncertainties linked to comparing the reference U-values and installing the test 
specimen. VELUX highlights potential inconsistencies arising from the tested roof window's manu-
facturing year (2008) versus the available data on windows from 2006 and 2009, potentially affecting 
reference comparisons. Moreover, the custom nature of the window complicates direct comparisons 
with off-the-shelf products, despite similarities. While both reference windows resemble the test win-
dow, differences may exist, such as Reference 1 (1.4 W/m²K) having more gas filling and an extra 
film layer compared to reference 2 (1.5 W/m²K), refer to Appendix A.15 for a more comprehensive 
description of the IGU. Additionally, variations in window size between the tested window (U08) and 
reference size (S08) could influence results, since larger sizes typically result in better U-values due 
to a higher glass proportion. Other factors like rounding numbers, installation depth, and measure-
ment equipment uncertainty in both the original report and recent product measurements may con-
tribute to discrepancies.  
  
 
Figure 36 Window subjected to hot box method (u-value), with cladding and tape (left), partly covered with cladding (right) 
Furthermore, thermal bridges within the specimen or at the interface with the hot box can distort heat 
flow patterns and compromise measurement accuracy. Identifying and mitigating these bridges is 
essential but can be difficult. Tape and sealant minimizing leakages, in places where the cladding was 
insufficient, could introduce additional uncertainties. These alterations displayed in Figure 36 may 
disrupt heat flow, affecting the accuracy of U-value measurements.

Chapter 7. Conclusion 
 
73 
 
Chapter 7. Conclusion 
7.1 
Conclusion on research hypotheses 
Hypothesis A: maintenance and recycling have a direct positive impact on a window's life cycle costs 
and environmental impacts. 
The examination of Hypothesis A, which suggests that maintenance and repurposing have a direct 
positive impact on a window's life cycle costs and environmental impacts, reveals nuanced insights 
into sustainable window options. Through the analysis of LCA and LCC, nuanced insights into the 
complex interplay between environmental sustainability, economic considerations, and performance 
have been elucidated. The findings underscore the importance of a balanced approach to sustainable 
construction practices, where project priorities and contextual factors play a pivotal role in decision-
making. While the utilization of recycled materials presents promising opportunities for reducing 
environmental footprints, careful consideration of maintenance implications is essential. Similarly, 
prioritizing maintenance and restoration offers cost-saving benefits but requires careful management 
to mitigate potential performance drawbacks. 
Hypothesis B: by testing the thermal performances of existing roof windows it is possible to identify 
the weakest components. 
The examination of the thermal performances of existing roof windows provided valuable insights 
into their integrity and energy efficiency, thus addressing Hypothesis B. Through comprehensive test-
ing, it became evident that thermal performance testing serves as an effective tool for identifying the 
weakest components within windows. By subjecting windows to various tests such as air permeability 
assessments and thermal transmittance, vulnerabilities in specific areas such as frames, seals, and 
thermal bridges have been uncovered. 
The results underscore the significance of regular maintenance and targeted refurbishment in address-
ing these vulnerabilities. By implementing specific interventions such as tightening frame screws and 
enhancing insulation, improvements in thermal efficiency have been demonstrated. The transition 
from Class A2 to Class A3 classification following basic maintenance exemplifies the potential for 
significant enhancements in energy performance. The findings indicate that the reclaimed window’s 
thermal transmittance performs as well as the reference windows, suggesting an overall acceptable 
thermal performance. This discovery suggests that used windows may possess a longer lifespan than 
commonly presumed, presenting a promising possibility for sustainable building practices. However, 
it is essential to acknowledge the multifaceted nature of thermal performance testing. While the study 
has provided valuable insights into the current state of window efficiency, certain limitations persist. 
Small sample sizes undergoing thermal performance testing, long-term durability, and performance 
degradation over extended periods remain areas of concern that necessitate ongoing maintenance and 
reassessment.  
In conclusion, the study offers valuable contributions to the field by elucidating the effectiveness of 
thermal performance testing in identifying weak components within existing windows. By leveraging 
==PAGE:88==
Chapter 7. Conclusion 
 
74 
 
these insights, stakeholders can make informed decisions to optimize energy efficiency and enhance 
the longevity of building components. Continued research and vigilance will be essential in ensuring 
sustained efficiency and resilience in the built environment amidst evolving environmental and oper-
ational conditions. 
Hypothesis C: the background and practices linked to different stakeholders in the building process 
will influence the nature of challenges and opinions related to the reuse of windows. 
Hypothesis C suggests that stakeholder backgrounds and practices influence opinions and challenges 
related to window reuse. The findings support this hypothesis, highlighting the significance of mainte-
nance practices and stakeholder perspectives in shaping the approach to window reuse. The need for 
streamlined testing methods and comprehensive quality standards is evident, underscoring the barri-
ers to the widespread adoption of reuse practices. Overcoming these challenges requires collaborative 
efforts and a cultural shift toward sustainability in the construction industry. By addressing practical 
hurdles and incentivizing reuse through cost-effective solutions, stakeholders can pave the way for a 
greener future in construction practices. 
 
7.2 
Overall conclusion 
In conclusion, the challenges facing the construction industry in adopting sustainable recycling prac-
tices, particularly concerning windows, are evident. The absence of quality standards and legislative 
support for recycled materials hinders their widespread adoption, compounded by practical chal-
lenges like disassembly complexities and data deficiencies. Additionally, cultural transitions and eco-
nomic considerations pose further obstacles to overcome. 
Sustainability necessitates a thorough examination of various factors, including the environmental 
benefits of recycled and reused materials in window construction. While these materials offer signif-
icant environmental advantages, they also entail increased maintenance requirements. Conversely, 
prioritizing maintenance and refurbishment may lead to lower life cycle costs but could potentially 
compromise thermal performance. Stakeholders emphasize the need for reduced energy demands if 
such practices are to be implemented on a broader scale. 
Therefore, achieving sustainability in building practices requires a balanced approach, where project 
priorities optimize choices among environmental impact reduction, cost-effectiveness, and thermal 
performance. Simultaneously, it demands regular assessment, documentation, and maintenance as 
critical practices for preserving window integrity and performance over time, ensuring the sustaina-
bility of building practices. Collaboration among stakeholders is paramount in overcoming these chal-
lenges and fostering a culture of sustainability in the construction industry.
==PAGE:89==
Chapter 8. Further research 
 
75 
 
Chapter 8. Further research 
There are opportunities for further research within this project. To deepen the understanding of the 
potential of reclaimed windows in construction practices, additional research and investigation are 
important. This section outlines the potential opportunities for further investigation. 
Firstly, broadening stakeholder engagement to include a wider field of architects, contractors, devel-
opers, and legislators will offer more diverse perspectives on prevailing attitudes, challenges, and 
opportunities associated with window recycling and reuse. Collaborating with entities such as the 
Social- og Boligstyrelsen could give valuable insights into evolving legislation regarding recycling, 
reuse, demolition, and resource management.  
Secondly, it is imperative to address a crucial aspect: the applicability of the findings to different 
types of windows. Specifically, it is essential to clarify that the results apply to primarily roof win-
dows and may not directly translate to facade windows. To determine whether facade windows exhibit 
similar behaviour, additional studies specifically focused on this window type are important. Addi-
tional valuable research would be to conduct a longitudinal study involving windows older than 15 
years. This would provide a comprehensive understanding of their performance over prolonged peri-
ods. An analysis spanning at least 20 to 25 years or more would yield nuanced insights into durability 
and maintenance requirements, crucial for assessing long-term viability. Furthermore, exploring the 
dynamic evolution of the solar energy transmittance (g-value) over the lifespan of windows would 
also yield valuable insights into its impact on thermal performance.  
Finally, investigating optimal storage conditions for reclaimed windows is paramount to mitigate 
degradation and preserve quality during temporary periods. Implementing controlled environmental 
storage solutions and preservation techniques will safeguard window integrity, ensuring suitability 
for future reuse. Measures to investigate the moisture content of windows before removal from build-
ings could also provide insights into preservation methods.
