i 
 
Appendices 
A.1. Environmental impact of windows materials life cycle phases 
Production phase: Glass, mainly consisting of sand and sodium, bears a substantial environmental 
impact, with raw material extraction contributing around 60% of greenhouse gas emissions [Analys-
ing the environmental impact of windows: A review]. Silica use, especially in processes like heat 
toughening, increases ecological impact. Spacers and seals have a minimal contribution of less than 
2%, while gas in insulated glass units sees increased embodied energy with improved thermal perfor-
mance [Analysing the environmental impact of windows: A review]. Frame materials, such as PVC, 
timber alu-cladding, and fiberglass, show varying impacts, with timber frames exhibiting the lowest 
values. The overall impact can be reduced through recycling, especially for timber frames, which 
demonstrate a low Global Warming Potential (GWP) during their long lifespan [Analysing the envi-
ronmental impact of windows: A review].  
Assembly phase: During the assembly or manufacturing phase, the environmental impact varies based 
on materials. Aluminium windows with double glazing contribute 10 pct to the Global Warming Po-
tential (GWP), while PVC windows with thermal bars and triple glazing can reach up to 45 pct [An-
alysing the environmental impact of windows: A review]. Material selection and manufacturing pro-
cesses are crucial in determining the overall environmental footprint [Analysing the environmental 
impact of windows: A review]. Material selection and manufacturing processes are crucial in deter-
mining the overall environmental footprint [Life cycle assessment (LCA) of windows and window 
materials]. 
Transport phase: The environmental impact during the transport phase depends on construction and 
distance. Efficient transportation methods and proximity to manufacturing sites are essential to min-
imize the carbon footprint associated with window transport [Analysing the environmental impact of 
windows: A review]. 
Use phase: The use phase significantly influences environmental impact, with various studies provid-
ing different answers [Analysing the environmental impact of windows: A review]. 
End-of-Life: End-of-life considerations vary based on modelling and allocation methods. While con-
tainer glass is frequently recycled, post-consumer float glass is rarely recycled [Analysing the envi-
ronmental impact of windows: A review]. Insulated glass units often end up in landfills, with minimal 
recycling. Frames made of timber and plastic are rarely recycled due to cost-effectiveness, while 
aluminium has a recovery rate of approximately 95 pct [Analysing the environmental impact of win-
dows: A review], phase [Life cycle assessment (LCA) of windows and window materials]. Addition-
ally, recycled aluminium consumes only about 10 pct of the energy of virgin aluminium, contributing 
to sustainability in the end-of-life phase [Life cycle assessment (LCA) of windows and window ma-
terials].

A.5. Detailed quality assurance from Miljøstyrelsen’s and Lendager's 
project 
Tests: The collected recycled windows undergo a series of tests before being approved for Krone 
UP Windows. These tests help determine their potential applications.  
Visual Inspection: During the processing of recycled windows, the glass is disassembled from the 
old frame. All recycled windows undergo a visual inspection for any scratches and damages. Super-
ficial scratches and colour variations in the glass are accepted, as they do not affect the functionality 
of the window. See point 10. Tolerances for further information. 
Glass Data: If Krone UP Windows is used as a climate screen, the windows are tested for U-value 
(insulation capability), g-value (solar factor), and LT-value (transmittance) - see Appendix C and D. 
Calculated average values for Upcycle Studios are as follows: 
• U-value: 0.69 (area-weighted average) 
• g-value: 0.49 
Durability: To assess the durability of recycled windows, a dew point analysis of all windows and a 
determination of dry matter content as samples have been conducted. Based on the tests performed 
on the windows, it is indicated that the windows have a lifespan that is on average comparable to 
new windows. There will be a sample control of the expected remaining lifespan of the windows in 
each window delivery. 
Energy Balance and Indoor Climate: 
• Recycled windows have been tested to ensure they meet the requirements for energy bal-
ance. 
• DVV: Krone UP Windows is DVV-marked, ensuring compliance with the standards for 
product and production defined in the Technical Specifications for Danish Window Verifica-
tion. 
• Energy Efficiency: By combining two window units into a double-framed Krone UP Win-
dow, the windows can meet the official guarantee for the energy efficiency of the window, 
as per DS/EN 10077-1 rules. This has been tested by the Technological Institute on three 
different designs of window systems for Krone UP Windows. 
• BR15/20 and Eref: Krone UP Windows, used as climate screens, have been tested and ap-
proved by the Technological Institute to comply with BR15. Additionally, the window con-
struction's ability to meet BR20 is calculated. Requirements for BR15 and BR20 include 
specific values: 
• BR20: Construction with insulating glass in the interior layer and energy glass in the exte-
rior layer. Eref: +7 kWh/m²K 
• BR15: Construction with insulating glass in both interior and exterior layers. Eref: -16 
kWh/m²K 
==PAGE:103==
 
ix 
 
CE Marking: New frames in Krone UP Windows are CE-marked by the manufacturer, while the fin-
ished window element is not CE-marked. 
Static Analysis: Double-frame elements supplied by "Lendager UP's" collaborator comply with cur-
rent requirements for BR20 for blower door tests and requirements for load and tightness for rain 
and wind. Bracing for wind load is not included in the product. 
Operable Sections: Openings are designed to achieve the recommended opening area according to 
applicable regulatory requirements. Opening of Krone UP Windows as double-frames occurs by 
opening a window in both the interior and exterior frame. The windows can be side- and top-hung 
depending on orientation and size. Opening mechanisms in both the interior and exterior frames en-
sure natural ventilation. For windows with direct access at height, the opening is done manually 
with an espagnolette handle. 
Emergency Openings: 
Krone UP Windows are designed to comply with applicable requirements for emergency openings 
specified in the specific project. 
Safety Glass: New safety glass is inserted into Krone UP Windows, or safety film is applied to recy-
cled glass where required. This decision is made by "Lendager UP" or the project-specific architect. 
The windows are equipped with safety glass - tempered or laminated safety glass - according to ap-
plicable safety glass requirements per DS/INF 119:2007. 
Noise Reduction: Krone UP Windows are made with sound glass according to applicable require-
ments for noise reduction. Windows must comply with the applicable requirements for sound, 
breakage, personal safety, and fire. Additional noise reduction needs can be found in section 10.5. 
Resin Irregularities: Irregularities in the surface treatment of timber frames may occur as resin 
knobs. Resin knobs can be randomly distributed on the surface or follow the grain pattern of the 
timber. Resin can also penetrate the paint film and form small drops on the surface. When the drops 
have been on the surface long enough to crystallize, they can be brushed off or lightly scraped off 
without compromising the surface treatment. Items with a high resin content may occur, and re-
moval of resin is the responsibility of the "Buyer."

A.8. Interview questions for each stakeholder type. 
Consultants: 
1. What are the biggest challenges you encounter when introducing recycled building materials 
as a sustainable practice in your projects? 
2. If you plan to use recycled building materials, where do you intend to source them from (e.g., 
material banks like Genbyg, demolishers, or directly from the construction site)? 
3. Do you consider design for disassembly in your projects, and if so, how do you implement it? 
4. How do you assess the quality of windows in your projects – are they often deteriorated or 
partly functional and suitable for reuse in new projects? 
5. Do you have any projects with recycled windows? 
Manufacturers (VELFAC): 
1. Which parts of windows can be made with recycled materials, and what are the major chal-
lenges associated with this? 
2. When producing new windows, do you focus on their potential for reuse, e.g., 50 years later, 
with a focus on design for disassembly? 
3. How do you manage and track hazardous substances in new, recycled, or reused materials? 
4. What is your outlook on the future of window reuse in the construction industry, and what 
trends or developments do you anticipate? 
5. Are there any legal or regulatory challenges or incentives affecting your work with window 
reuse? 
Social housing: 
1. What is your experience with using refurbished recycled windows? 
2. How do you view the potential benefits and drawbacks of highly recyclable windows and 
directly reused windows in social housing projects? 
3. Have you ever considered using recycled windows in your projects, or windows with high 
recycled content? 
4. What do you see as the biggest challenges of reusing windows, and which are the most sig-
nificant? 
5. Do you consider the environmental impact of the products you procure, and would you be 
willing to invest in highly recyclable windows even if they might be slightly more expensive? 
Demolishers: 
==PAGE:108==
 
xiv 
 
1. Can you describe the challenges arising from rules and standards in the construction industry 
regarding material reuse and recycling? 
2. How do variations in legislation affect the categorization and handling of materials like win-
dows on construction sites, especially when they do not fit into a specific waste category? 
3. What are common methods for categorizing materials for reuse on construction sites based on 
factors like quantity, volume, weight, and type? 
4. Have you observed any new potentials, practices, or standards addressing the challenge of 
quantifying and categorizing materials for potential reuse, especially for items like windows? 
5. Can you share examples of successful initiatives or projects where materials like windows 
were successfully identified and separated for reuse, and how was this achieved? 
Brancheorganisationer (Vinduesindustrien): 
1. What are the primary technical, professional, and business challenges currently facing the 
window industry regarding the reuse and renovation of windows? 
2. How do you plan to advise your members and others on reuse and recycling to help them 
make the correct decisions? 
3. How will circular construction change the way windows are designed compared to the current 
approach, including their composition, materials, and content? 
4. Are there specific requirements, either legal or technical, that window manufacturers should 
consider to ensure their windows are suitable for reuse or renovation? 
5. What methods do you recommend for assessing the quality of recycled or reused windows to 
ensure they meet the necessary standards and specifications? 
Teknologisk institut (forhenværende medarbejder hos Velux): 
1. What is your insight regarding material passports or resource mapping concerning the reuse 
and recycling of building materials? 
2. Can you provide examples of innovative technologies or methods that can contribute to im-
proving the recycling process for windows? 
3. How do you assess the quality and lifespan of recycled windows, and what factors are crucial 
in this regard? 
4. Do you have any idea which parts of the window components deteriorate first? 
5. What do you see as the greatest challenges and barriers to increasing the use of reused win-
dows? 
Arkitekter og industriel design: 
1. Can you share specific projects or experiences where you have reused windows? 
==PAGE:109==
 
xv 
 
2. What specific challenges have you faced when it comes to window reuse, and how have you 
addressed them? 
3. What innovative approaches or design solutions have you implemented to improve the win-
dow recycling process? 
4. Can you describe the process, such as parameters and considerations, which were crucial to 
ensuring the windows could be reused? 
5. Are there any specific criteria or standards you have used to ensure the quality of reused win-
dows? 
Interview with the Environmental Protection Agency: 
1. What role do municipalities play as building authorities in determining whether reused build-
ing materials comply with the Building Regulations, especially when technical requirements 
are not met? 
2. Can you elaborate on the typical criteria or considerations that a municipal authority considers 
when assessing such exemption requests? What are the key factors? 
3. How does national legislation regulate the marketing and sale of building materials without 
CE marking, which are still covered by the Construction Products Regulation? 
4. How does the regulation address the challenge of integrating reused or recycled materials 
within these basic requirements? 
5. Are there any specific initiatives or guidelines for professionals to ensure that reused materials 
without performance declarations are suitable for their purpose?

Sources of error 
To ensure the applicability of the results, elements such as industry-specific barriers and cultural factors, and the changing time should be 
considered. While utilizing an inductive approach and open-ended questions allows for a more organic exchange of information, it also brings 
into question the consistency of the data. The open-ended questions make it difficult to maintain consistency in the data since the responses 
can vary across the different phases of the building life cycle. It can also be harder to identify categories within the responses, while the 
interpretation may be influenced by the researcher’s perspective, introducing a potential bias.  
Another potential bias may be the stakeholder selection. Selecting stakeholders primarily from specific groups poses a risk of excluding 
diverse perspectives, resulting in an incomplete understanding of the relevant issues. To mitigate the potential bias industry associations such 
as Vinduesindustrien and Træinformation were included since they can provide a broader overview of the industry. Careful consideration 
ensures a nuanced understanding of the sector's diversity and complexity. Despite implementing various measures to prevent potential bias 
in this study, bias could still have occurred. In the next sections (5.2.2 to 5.2.7) different perspectives from the companies will be discussed.  
==PAGE:120==
 
xxvi 
 
A.10. 
Stakeholder aspects of material reuse in the building industry 
Company 
Main points 
Tscherning 
1. Points out issues with regulations designed for new products, noting their economic 
implications and the need for adaptation when dealing with existing structures. 
2. They highlight, that regulatory models, while reasonable, may not seamlessly apply to 
existing structures, requiring adjustments. 
3. Emphasizes the importance of cultural awareness and the need to assist field colleagues 
in preserving the value of materials during removal, preventing them from being dis-
carded. 
4. Stresses challenges in early decision-making for material choices in new construction 
projects. Advocates for improved decision-making regarding reused materials, focusing 
on material quantity and potential resale value. 
HusCompagniet 
1. Expresses concerns about the lack of data for used windows, emphasizing the need for 
A-rating, appearance considerations, and data to ensure customer satisfaction. 
2. Highlights the importance of maintaining guarantees and ensuring the same level of 
quality for reused windows to prevent customer complaints and uphold satisfaction. 
3. Stresses the significance of a reliable construction process, with a focus on delivery 
assurance to ensure that ordered windows meet specifications in terms of size, colour, 
and other factors. 
4. Discusses the lack of economic incentives for sustainability, noting the relatively low 
cost of producing waste compared to the marginal savings in sorting. Identifies a need 
for stronger economic motivations. 
DOVISTA 
1. Acknowledges the need to reconsider current standards and proposes potential flexibil-
ity, allowing adjustments based on the environmental superiority of recycled materials. 
2. Explores opportunities in projects with strong client relationships, suggesting a window 
take-back system where customers can calculate discounts for exchanging their old win-
dows. 
3. Faces challenges in deciding on an appropriate third party for collaboration, questioning 
whether they should retrieve old windows themselves or rely on others. 
4. Acknowledges the cultural shift required and emphasizes the need for operational 
changes, particularly in optimizing truck setups for secure glass transportation and ad-
dressing legal and product lifespan issues. 
Vinduesindustrien 
1. Recycling some windows is difficult due to legal requirements when repurposing them 
in other buildings. 
2. Glass recycling poses challenges in terms of energy costs, weight, and technical diffi-
culties. The process involves melting old glass and reforming it into a new, potentially 
more efficient window with added layers. 
3. Older windows may contain hazardous paint or treated timber, making their recycling 
technically challenging. Custom measurements and designs of older windows also con-
tribute to the complexity. 
4. Recycling, while environmentally beneficial, is economically more expensive. Higher 
glass recycling percentages can be rewarded in environmental declarations, encourag-
ing efforts to reduce the carbon footprint of windows. 
BOSJ 
1. Struggles with stringent documentation and quality requirements for recycled compo-
nents. 
2. Aims to collaborate with suppliers for "take-back" programs, transferring circularity 
responsibility to manufacturers. 
3. Ongoing questions about responsibilities and guarantees among contractors, builders, 
and manufacturers. 
==PAGE:121==
 
xxvii 
 
4. Emphasizes the need to balance aesthetics, economics, and environmental benefits. 
Urges early incorporation of reuse principles in planning to avoid later economic chal-
lenges. 
COWI 
1. COWI faces overarching difficulties related to documentation, guarantee provision, and 
the demolition process, extending beyond windows. 
2. Identifies a lack of data on various aspects of materials, emphasizing the need for data 
integration and standards to support transparency and successful recycling practices. 
3. Highlights the significant potential for reuse in 80-90% of building materials but 
stresses the requirement for new processes, methods, and extended lifespans through 
warranties to activate this potential. 
4. Advocates for designing structures for disassembly, avoiding glued materials, and pro-
moting the standardization of separable materials. Additionally, anticipates future tech-
nology, emphasizing the need to retain materials for future use to prevent substantial 
resource loss. 
Træinfo 
1. Provide timber construction instructions with a focus on window details and thermal 
bridge reduction, adhering to BR18 requirements. 
2. Acknowledge the challenge of applying instructions to used windows. 
3. Highlight the absence of guidelines for secondary windows and the potential impacts 
of adding an extra glass layer, prompting the need for a life cycle assessment (LCA). 
4. Emphasize the use of screws in assembly, allowing for theoretical disassembly, and 
express a commitment to prioritize designing for disassembly in future updates. 
Teknologisk Institut 
1. Identifies a time-related challenge with material passports, noting that older buildings 
may have passports that become outdated due to maintenance, painting, and renova-
tions. 
2. Stresses the need to extend the lifespan of high-quality products for true circular econ-
omy and sustainability, emphasizing the importance of reusing older components for 
secondary constructions. 
3. Points out limitations in reusing facade windows due to the custom nature of measure-
ments and ordering, as sizes are often determined after on-site measurement and may 
not be standardized. 
4. Highlights potential issues in the longevity of triple-glazed windows compared to dou-
ble-glazed ones, noting that the former may lose their filling or insulation capacity more 
quickly due to their construction. 
5. Advocates for standards, particularly quality standards, to guide the reuse of building 
materials. Suggests specifying important parameters and test methods for assessing the 
quality of reused products, promoting consistency and reliability. 
Glaseksperten 
1. Identifies purity as a major challenge in recycled glass, emphasizing the need to address 
issues related to its cleanliness. 
2. Highlights the importance of proper sorting into correct fractions and the need for trace-
ability in the recycling process. 
3. Highlights the need for enough desiccant in spacers to increase the lifespan of windows.  
4. Addresses the proven issue of gas diminishing in windows over time. While theoreti-
cally refillable, practicality and cost-effectiveness present limitations. Emphasizes the 
critical role of spacers and the need for sufficient desiccants to prevent window punc-
tures. 
KAB 
1. Increasing energy efficiency standards pose challenges for direct reuse of windows, as 
older ones may not meet the latest requirements. 
2. Advisors lack readiness for using recycled materials, reflecting an immature market 
where consultants struggle to integrate reuse solutions. 
==PAGE:122==
 
xxviii 
 
3. is a crucial factor in the affordable housing sector, influencing decisions regarding win-
dow reuse and replacements. 
4. Emphasizes the importance of maintaining windows for direct reuse, suggesting that 
careful removal and reinstallation within the same community can be beneficial. 
5. Large-scale reuse projects face logistical complexities, especially when moving win-
dows between different projects, here quality and documentation requirements add 
challenges. 
6. Raises concerns about allocating renewed risks for the lifespan of reused components. 
Building sector insurance entities may need to adapt to ensure recycled building ele-
ments that don't meet new product standards. 
7. Proposes a strategy of reserving parts from certain windows to act as spares for discon-
tinued but still viable windows, allowing for continued use even after their production 
has ceased. 
Flock Studios 
1. Using post-industrial scrap glass (rejected due to defects) combined with new timber 
frames is the most sustainable option when manufacturing windows with recycled con-
tent. 
2. Architects need an understanding of available sizes and design accordingly. 
3. Strict requirements are imposed, and existing materials may not meet energy standards, 
posing challenges in retrofitting. 
4. There is a need to challenge certain limits as current standards may render some build-
ing materials unusable. 
5. Significant investment in recycled glass panels faced challenges due to limited interest, 
prompting the exploration of alternative sources like post-production glass. 
Søren Jensen 
1. Recycled materials vary in form and expression, presenting uncertainties and concerns 
about environmentally hazardous substances. 
2. Some demolishers effectively manage recycled materials, while others lack control, 
making it challenging for builders to confidently use materials with unknown origins. 
3. Suggests screening early to understand the supply, availability, type, quality, and 
lifespan of materials, addressing environmental risks within bigger portfolios. 
4. Highlights the ease of using certain windows, such as thermal windows functioning as 
internal partitions, but emphasizes the preference for direct recycling. 
5. Advocates for labelling recycled materials to prevent loss or misinterpretation, empha-
sizing the importance of proper handling and documentation. 
6. Acknowledges the need for well-defined logistics for recycled materials, emphasizing 
adherence to buildability rules and increased attention during the recycling process. 
Miljøstyrelsen 
1. Material removal from buildings is instantly labelled as waste, posing challenges for 
recycling processes. 
2. New legislation targets selective demolition, prioritizing reuse over waste categoriza-
tion for larger buildings. 
3. New legislation targets buildings that must undergo resource screening for effective 
integration of treated reuse materials back into the product category. 
4. Emphasis on thorough material analysis instead of automatic waste labelling, encour-
aging a nuanced approach.

Interview transcriptions (in Danish)
